Yemen:
Sana'a Water Supply and Sanitation Project
Environmental Impact Assessment: Executive Summary
1. The Project Scope and Introduction
Tius document summanses the environmental impact assessment, environmental management plan and
social studces done for selected water and sewer mvestments planned for the City of Sana'a The
portion of these investments to be financed by IDA, under the proposed Sana'a Water Supply and
Samtatiorn Project, have been categonsed by the World Bank as a Category "B" project for the
purposes of thls assessment
2. The Project Description and Setting
The GOY has a comprehensive strategy for the development of water and wastewater services in the
City of Sana'a These are specified m the "Sana'a Water Supply and Samtation Services
Development Progranmmne, Draft Final Report, October 1998" The most immedcate of the planned
mvestments under the Development Programme are generally referred to as "Sana'a 1" and include the
items discussed below and whlch will be financed with the assistance of IDA's Sana'a Water Supply
and Samtation Project Investments that will follow Sana'a I are usually referred to as the "Long-
Term Programmre"
Investments under the Development Programme will proceed m at least two phases, startng with
Sana'a 1, which consists mamly of the Akamah Sewage component and the Hasabah Water Supply
Rehabilhtation component Sewer mvestments under the Long-Term Program for the city mnclude
mvestments m the Safiyah East, Saba'een, and Sawad Hanash areas ofthe city.
The Environmental Impact Assessment and Environmental Management Plan descnbed m this volume
covers. (i) Sana'a 1 (financed by IDA under the proposed Sana'a Water Supply and Saintation
Project), and (u) the three areas descnbed in the previous paragraph for the Long-Term Progranmne
IDA's Sana'a Water Supply and Samtaton Project would consist of the followmg components Water
Distnbution Leak detection and rehabilitation of existng pnmary, secondary and tertiary networks in
the Hasabah section of Sana'a Sewerage Extension of the sewer network to the Akamah area,
construction of a new sewer trunk main. Water Resources. Studies, surveys, and exploratory well-
drilling Lumited rehabilitation of existing wellfields and drillmg of a selected number of productive
wells Techmcal Assistance and Inshtutional Capacity Buildmg Establishment of an autonomous water
and waste-water corporation, enviromnental managemnent plan, TA and studies to support sector refonn
(mcludmg tariff studies), O&M enhancement studies, and the award of a pnvate sector operations and
mnanagement contract or lease for the Sana'a Branch of NWSA
Sana'a I and the Long-Term Progranme are set m the City of Sana'a, in established and built up
areas All the proposed civil works will take place in dedicated utility rights-of-ways.
Akamah
The Akainah Project area covers some 145 hectares and will house around 47,500 people at a relatively
high residential density, (in the context of Sana'a) of around 280 persons per hectare (pph). It typically
compnses low quality housing developments but also contains two mam commercia streets.
Y9701                                     -1-                                  March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
The system is designed as a separate system, i.e. for sewerage only, not a combined storm drainage and
sewerage system. Some 3,000 houses will be connected to a new sewer via about 30 kms of 150 mm
uPVC pipes running from private inspection chambers to a collection chamber then to the nearest
manhole or sewer line. An additional 2,000 households may be connected to the new sewer network
after the close of IDA's project. The project will potentially remove 2,900 cesspits in the Akamah area.
It also includes construction of about 4 kms of sewer trunk main ranging in diameter from 600 to 900
mm.
Hasabah:
The 325 hectare Hasabah area is one of the more densely populated areas in Sana'a, but is projected to
have a density in the year 2000 of only 147 pph. The ultimate density of 200 pph is projected to be
reached by year 2005. No further population infill can be accommodated. The total population to be
served is therefore estimated at 65,000.
This component of the project comprises the rehabilitation or replacement of large portions of the water
network in the Hasabah area. The project envisages about 27 kilometers of pipe to be replaced,
including portions of the primary, secondary and tertiary network. Some 20,000 house connections will
be added in the area during the analysis period and about 6,000 existing house connections and meters
will be replaced.
3. The Environmental Review and Assessment
The Environmental Review and Assessment
The proposed IDA project has been reviewed, screened, and designated for Category B consistent with
the provisions of the World Bank Group's Operational Directive 4.01, Annex E, "Environmental
Assessment". There are no major environmental issues involved. Further, there are no land
acquisition, involuntary resettlement, indigenous people or cultural heritage issues. The Project has no
effect on international waterways. The Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA) and Management Plan
was prepared by the Borrower with the assistance of international consultants and comprises three
elements:
* Project Description and Statement of Baseline Conditions
* Impact Statement
• Environmental Management Plan
In the EIA, extensive use is made of two household and commercial establishment surveys undertaken
as part of project preparation activities (summaries included in the Project File).
Major Environmental Issues and Mitigation Measures
The project has three main environmental issues, none of which are considered major or which will
cause irreparable harm to the enviromnent after the application of the proposed mitigation measure.
These issues are: (i) cesspit removal, and disposal of cesspit sewage during the initial months of
construction; (ii) the disposition of dried sewage sludge; and (iii) the handling of chemicals used to
flush rehabilitated or new water lines.
Cesspit Ranoval and DisDosal of Sewa=e:
Descnption: Before the new wastewater treatment plant is operational in December 1999, a maximum
of 600 cesspits will need to be destroyed during the initial phase of civil works under Sana'a 1. This
Y9701                                    -2-                                 March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services          Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
represents about 90 m3 per day of wastewater, for a maximum of about 9,000 m3 for the four months.
This sewage will be trucked to the existing pond wastewvater treatment plant for disposal. This existing
plant is often over-loaded and, therefore, not achieving design standards for treatment. However, this
waste will only contribute a maximum additional load of 1.5% of design capacity of 6,000 m3. Given
the desire to remove raw sewage from the crowded Akamah residential and commercial neighbourhood
as soon as possible, it is considered the better alternative to remove the sewage and carry it to the
existing pond treatment plant than to wait for the opening of the new wastewater treatment facility.
This is especially the case in light of the modest sewage quantities involved and the planned mitigation
program described below.
Mitigation: Prior to construction, community leaders will be advised of the details of work in their
area. Measures to prevent casual public access to the site will be undertaken, with particular emphasis
on children. Open pits in the construction areas will be covered at night in such a way that they cannot
be casually uncovered. During the first four months of the project, liquid wastes will be transported to,
and disposed into, the existing pond WWTP. Given that this treatment plant is already operating at or
over capacity during peak hours, wastes will be transferred in off-peak hours. Site supervisors will be
present at both the construction and treatment plant to register vehicles, and to ensure that unauthorised
dump sites are not used. Monitoring of coliform levels in the ponds will be undertaken to ensure that
health in the area of the WWTP is not being endangered. Aeration devises may be used, if feasible, to
improve treatment effectiveness.
After the initial four-month period, i.e., by December 1999, the new WWTP is expected to be
operational. It will easily handle all wastes from the destruction of the remaining approximately 2,300
cesspits. Operating specifications and capacities of the new plant are included as an Appendix C to the
EIA.
Dried Sewage Sludze:
Description: The new wastewater treatment plant will produce about 32 tons per day of sludge with a
30 percent moisture content. The plant site has a storage area that will accommodate approximately 15
months of accumulation. The government currently has no policy on the use of dried sludge and has
not identified a final dumping site or other disposition measures.
Miigation: Dried sludge from the existing WWTP is being sold to local farmers, who compost it, and
there is enough demand to dispose of 80,00 tons per year. However, a fomial wastewater and sludge
reuse policy will be developed with assistance from IDA as part of the Project (see Environmental
Mitigation component of the Project).
Disposal of Hazardous Chemicals:
Description: The use of small amounts of hazardous chemicals will be used as part of the normal
cleaning procedure during the final stages of the water system rehabilitation component. The Project
involves the installation or rehabilitation of 27 kilometres of pipe, ranging in size from 80mm to
600mm, and the amount of chemicals necessary for flushing is expected to be modest. Pipes will be
flushed with water containing 40-50 ppm chlorine gas over a 24-hour period.
Mitigation: The contractor will be resposible for training workers on the handling of such chemicals,
and providing suitable safety gear. Spill prevention and counter-control rneasures for hazardous
chemicals will be specified in a contingency plan (included in the EIA Appendix). Spills of hazardous
materials will be promptly cleaned up and chemically treated. Workers handling these spills will wear
overalls and gloves in addition to any other protective equipment that may be needed. Wastewater
containing hazardous chemicals will be routed to the new wastewater treatment plant and the feed rate
Y9 701                                   -3-                                 March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services          Sana a I Environmental Impact Assessment
of hazardous chemicals limited to levels that can be handled by the plant. No flushing of new water
lines will be undertaken before the new WWTP is operational. Hazardous chemical treatment training
will be provided to wastewater treatment plant staff. Finally, compliance with the proposed provisions
will be monitored by an Environment and Safety Officer (ESO) in the PMU.
Secondary Environmental Issues and Mitigation Measures
Emplovee Health and Safetv:
Soil in project areas will be sampled to test for contamination. In the case of minimal risk, simple
mitigation measures will consist of wearing appropriate clothing, and education of workers. In the case
of more severe contamination, waste material will be excavated and taken to an appropriate NWSA and
EPC approved site.
Public Safetv:
A number of public health and safety measures are addressed to limit risk of an accident involving the
public. These would include erecting appropriate fences and walkways to permit safe passage of
pedestrian and vehicular traffic in and around the site. On-site inspectors will supervise heavy
equipment use.
Public Health:
As part of the public infonnation campaign, the population shall be advised as to the nature and extent
of the works proposed. Capacities of local hospitals and clinics will be assessed and, if deemed
necessary, a project clinic will be established in project areas without easy access to existing facilities.
Collectio Stora-ge and Disuosal of Household Wastewater:
The contractor will establish a special unit to specifically handle this task. This unit will dispose of the
wastewater in a manner that prevents spillage of wastewater. All such wastes will be transported to the
inlet sump of the WWTP, and will not be handled by any workers outside of the designated unit.
Abatement of Noise and Air Pollution:
A detailed plan for the control of noise pollution is included in the EA. The measures to be undertaken
will include proper maintenance of equipment, erection of screens, use of muffling devices to minimise
both dust and noise pollution, and strict monitoring of vehicular traffic used in project works. Noise and
dust levels will be kept within recommended World Bank and Yemeni standards.
Public Consultation Process:
Despite the fact that this type of category '1B" environmental project does not require a public
consultation program, the Govermnent of Yemen has nevertheless implemented an extensive program of
public awareness and consultation. This program comprises three components: (i) public meetings; (ii)
newspaper and television articles and programs; and (iii) household surveys. Each of these are
summarised below:
Public Meetings: To date there have been 3 public meetings about the project. The first was on 26 July
1997, and the latest took place on 20 February 1999. Another meeting is scheduled before the start of
construction, and at least semi-annual meetings are planned during th csuctio odpeig4 Q-
average, these public meetings are attended by about 30 to 35 people living in the affected
Y9701                                    -4-                                 March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services           Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
neighbourhoods. Topics discussed typically include the deterioration of sewerage conditions, the
duration of construction, and the number of households served.
Newspaper and Television Coveraze: To date, there have been 5 newspaper articles about the project.
The first appeared on 22 May 1998, and the most recent was on 16 November 1998. In general, the
articles discuss sewerage problems, what the project will do to alleviate these problems, and reports on
the status of the project. On 25 October 1997, the PMU Director for the Project, among others, were
on a live television show discussing potential impacts of the projects' works, and answering questions
on how to facilitate the construction process.
Household Surveys: Characteristics of the two household surveys undertaken prior to the start of the
project are summarised in the following table:

Results of the surveys show that households consider street flooding from cesspit overflow to be the
most important enviromnental consideration in their neighbourhoods. Household respondents also
expressed a strong preference for a conventional water bome sewer system as the remedy for this
situation. The Project plans a follow-up survey in the Akamah area halfway through its
implernentation penod, which is estimated to be one year after the sewage network has been completed.
Overall Cost of the Project's Environmental Program
A sumnmary of the Project's environmental issues, mitigation program, and associated costs is
summarised in Table E.2. For Sana'a 1, the cost is expected to be around $230,000. This includes the
salary of the ESO, the cost of all mitigation measures and monitoring, the cost of a new standby
generator for the old wastewater treatment plant, and the cost of a sludge and wastewater re-use study.
Overall mitigation costs are expected to be around $350,000.
Conclusions
The overall impact of the Project is to significantly improve environmental conditions at every phase of
the project cycle, but particularly in the Akamah area. Until completion of the new WWTP,
environmnental impacts from the disposal of cesspit sewage at the existing WWTP are epected to be
minimal, and any adverse impact is outweighed by the immnediate improvemnents in the Akamah area.
The environmental review concludes that the proposed project will generate overwhelmingly positive
environmental, social and economic impacts. With its migation measures, the project does not present
negative environmental consequences. Detailed discussion of all items, including the complete
mitigation plan and item-by-item costs, are included in the EIA.

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services       Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
1.       INTRODUCTION
1.1      PROJECT BACKGROUND AND OBJECTIVES
This Report has been prepared for the Sana'a Water Supply and Sanitation Project (SWSS),
Contract Addendum No. 1. The addendum for this IDA supported sub-project was signed
between NWSA and Dar Al-Handasah Consultants (Shair and Partners) in March 1998 and
the project commenced in March 1998
This project is done in Sana'a, in established and built up areas. The project will proceed in
two main phases, starting with Sana'a 1, which consists mainly of the Akamah Sewage Project
and the Hasabah Water Supply Rehabilitation Project. Sana'a 1 is part of the much larger
Sana'a Water Supply and Sanitation Project (SWSS). The second phase, commonly referred
to as the Long-Term Program, includes the Safiyah East Sewage Project, the Saba'een
Sewage Project, and the Sawad Hanash Sewage Project.
1.2      REPORT SCOPE
This document comprises the Environmental Impact Assessment for the defined works. It is
prepared under the guidelines provided by the WB in their OD 4.01 for a Category B Project.
The project category being defined in the addendum.
This report therefore comprises three basic elements:
-     Project Description and Statement of Baseline Conditions
-     Imnpact Statement
-     Environmental Manageiment Plan (EMP)
AUl the civil works take place in dedicated utility rights-of-ways. All projects within the Long-
Term Program are accommodated in the EIA though there is a clear emphasis on the Sana'a 1
projects.
Extensive use is made of the surveys undertaken as part of the SAWAS and Sana'a Water
Supply and Sanitation Project (SWSS) projects and the World Bank's survey' (ESS) of the
affected communities, to attempt to quantify project envirornental costs and benefits.
1.3       REPORT STRUCTURE
The structure of the report reflects the Scope of the works undertaken defined above. It
contains six firther sections as described below.
The legislative and institutional frameworks for the project and the environmental sector as a
whole are summarised in Section 2.
l The Economics of Sanitation: A Case Study of Saru'a. In preparationL Data obtained from Draft
docu mentation.

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services        Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
Section 3 provides a general description of the projects including a brief overview of project
objectives and need. Existing environmental conditions including a review of socio-economic
conditions are summarised in Section 4.
Project Impacts, both positive and negative are outlined in Section 5.
The remaining Sections, 6 and 7 comprise the EMP. Section 6 contains the Mitigation Plan
developed in direct response to ameliorate the significant negative impacts outlined in the
previous section. Section 7 describes the proposed Project Monitoring works.
The final Section contains the proposals for EMP Implementation. These include:
-     Programme Responsibilities and Implementation Structure
-     Reporting Schedules
-     An Implementation Schedule
-     EMP cost estimates

2.       ENVIRONMENTAL APPRAISAL FRAMEWORK
2.1      INTRODUCTION
This Section provides an outline of the existing framework for the execution of Environmental
Appraisal works in Yemen.
Section 2.2 deals with the institutional framework for EAs. Specifically, the identification of
agencies, governmental and non-governmental, that have responsibilities relating to the
preparation, supervision and management of EAs and, or, that may contribute to the review
process. This is followed by three further Sections, 2.3, to 2.5 that outline, respectively, the
legislative, policy, and financial frameworks supporting the defined institutional structure.
The final, Section 2.6, outlines other projects and programmes in Yemen, which contain
Environmental, Water, and/or Sanitation components.
2.2      INSTITUTIONAL FRAMEWORK
2.2.1     Governmental Organisations
A.        Primary Authority
Prior to 1995 primary responsibility for environmental matters in Yemen resided with the State
Ministry of Environmental Affairs, although an Environmental Protection Council (EPC) was
established in 1990 under Prime Ministerial decree 94. This council was reformed by the Prime
Minister's decree (No. 28) of 1995, to perform the tasks outlined in Table 2.1.
After 1995, and as a result of the reforms brought about by the enaction of the Environmental
Protection Law (Law No. 26 of 1995), (EPL), the EPC became the government agency with
primary responsibility for environrental matters.
Under Article 5 of the EPL the EPC is required to
undertake the preparation of the general national policy for environment protection and its
control and shall carry out in co-ordination with concerned bodies to execute such policy
after approval of the cabinet. Each concerned body shall adhere and be obliged to implement
the decisions, resolutions and recommendations that are issued by the counciL
Accordingly, the EPC has, primarily, an oversight and management function with many
responsibilities remaining with existing authorities. To facilitate this oversight role, the EPC is
established as one of three entities under the direct control of the Cabinet and its annual
budget is part of the budget of the Council of Ministers.
EPC Structure
The EPC itself comprises the 11 members identified in Table 2.2.
Y9701                                 -2.1-                            March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services               Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
Table 2.1  Functions of the EPC'
* Propose the general policy to protect the environment and adopt environmental design research as the
general framework for an adequate solution of pollution problems
* Co-ordination of concerned national agencies and follow up and assess their activities as well as co-
ordination with regional and international organisations concerned with the protection of the environment
* Set up standards that are suitable for protecting the environment from pollution and make use of scientific
principles in this respect to assure the abatement of pollution and the protection of the animal, plant and
marine ecology etc.
* Prepare draft laws regulations and systems necessazy to protect the environment and review regional and
international agreements on environmental protection.
* Set up effective programmes for international co-operation and enviromuental protection for
implementation in accordance with general GOY policy
* Collect data, carry out assessments and prepare reports on the status of the environment, and establish
monitoring systems capable
* Set up the general framework of environment education and awareness programmes with the objective of
enhancing awareness among the public and inducing them individually and collectively to participate in the
protection of the environment.
* Record and evaluate changes in the condition of the enviromnent and Natural Resources of Yemen and
inform the Cabinet of these changes by means of regular reports
* Prepare a national plan for training of a Yemeni cadre in the various environment fields
* Organise local seminars and deliver lectures concerning environment protection as well as represent the
ROY at interational conferences and seminars and conduct bilateral meetings regarcng such issues.
Source: EPC 1997

under the Prime Minister's decree, No. 34. In effect, the administering of the EIA process and
any other requirements and procedures included in the EIA legislation. This involves not only
the drafting of EIA guidelines, standards and regulations but also the implementation of the
EIA Process.

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services            Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
Unfortunately, the reality is that few if any of these pre existing 'environmental' national
NGOs can be considered truly effective at present, or potentially, as principal players in the
implementation of a sector strategy in the future. There are a number of reasons for this.
First, and most importantly, many of the NGOs are in fact quasi-government agencies and
therefore can not, and are not, viewed as independent bodies by the population. The 1963
YAR Law No. 1 13 still provides the legal framework for the establishment, organisation,
registration of organisations and clubs of a cultural, social and charitable nature.
While the right of citizens to form NGOs is recognised in the constitution of Yemen4, the
stipulations of Law 11 are restrictive in a number of ways. Two specific examples provided
below illustrate how potentially pervasive government control of an organisation could be.
Law No. 11 specifies:
-     that NGOs must have a board of directors, a general assembly and election procedures,
but also specifies that the meetings of the general assembly beginning from the day of
foundation must be monitored by a representative of the Ministry of Social Affairs
(MOILSA today) who must sign the minutes.
-     that MOILSA has the right to take control of, or abort the society whenever this is
deemed necessary.
In the former case MOILSA may also abolish a society or freeze its activities merely through
an administrative decision to reject or not response to an application for, or renewal of a
license or simply by failing to dispatch a representative to a meeting.
Though the law clearly provides the opportunity for government interference in NGO
activities, it need not necessarily do so. However, the unevenness and seemingly arbitrary
application of lawss, either by design or accident implies that this is not the case. Many
agencies function effectively believing that they can only do so through government patronage,
thereby breeding public distrust.
2.3    LEGISLATIVE FRAMEWORK
2.3.1      General Framework
The EPL is now the framework environmental legislation for Yemen. In general terms it
provides for the controlling of actions or activities described as being 'unsafe to the
environment'. Specific provisions in that act that may relate directly to this project are
identified in Table 2.6.
3 A Draft NGO law is under study by MOLSA. It is understood that this draft has been prepared with little
or no input from NGOs themselves and that it effectively establishes tighter control over the activities of
NGOs than the exwsting Law. It is considered unlikely therefore that any of the dscussion provided will be
made redundant by the promulgation of a decree establishing the new law.
4 Articles 5, 6, 7, 14, 27, 32, 34, and 41 of the Amended Constitution of September 22th 1994
S For example in the case of licensing: Licensing periods often vary and may depend on the influence of the
founders, some NGOs operate without a license, some are not required to apply for license renewal.

this draft legislation does no include any measures that vary significantly from the initial
documentation prepared with the assistance of Euroconsult in 1996.

2.4    POLICY FRAMEWORK
The National Environmental Action Plan (NEAP)6 has been prepared with the support of the
UNDP and World Bank and was published in 1996.The principal elements of the NEAP as
they affect the Water Supply and Sanitation Sectors are summarised in Table 2.8.
While the NEAP does not have any legal, it provides the policy framework for the actions of
the EPC. As such, it provides guidance on the application or otherwise of the available
legislation though, in Yemen, as in most countries, the existing body of legislation (prior to the
EPL) was probably sufficient to provide adequate protection to the environment had there
been an effective policy framework for its application.
Table 2.8: Principal Elements of the NPE Affecting the Water and Sanitation Sectors
Sector / Issue                  Concern
Water Depletion, Pollution and Supply Over extraction of groundwater
Lack of water allocation and conservation systems
Water Pollution
Inadequate Water supply Services
Land Degradation                Soil Erosion
Waste Management                Wastewater management
Solid Waste Management
Hazardous Waste management
_  Pesticide Management
Source: NEAP, 1996
2.5    FINANCING FRAMEWORK
Article 92 of the EPL (1995) stipulates that an Environmental Protection Fund (YEP) is
established to receive all moneys allocated by the GOY to protect the enviromnent. This fund
has now been established and is managed on a day to day basis by the Chairman of the EPC,
or more usually his delegated authority.
Table 2.9 summarises the overall structure of the YEP, its potential sources of revenue,
eligible beneficiaries and eligible projects
It is not a function of this study to assess the adequacy or otherwise of the funds allocated to
the YEP, or the effectiveness of their use. Suffice to say that discussion with the EPC and
others active in the environmental arena imply that the funds available are generally insufficient
for EPC to effectively perform the actions with which it is tasked in the EPL.
6 The EPL Article 5 (2) requires the EPC to prepare a National Environmental Action Plan; the official
organ of the state and shall undertake the preparation of the general national policyfor environment
protection and its control.
The NEAP is thus considered to be established element of the Environmental Framework.

3.        PROJECT DESCRIPTIONS
3.1       INTRODUCTION
This section contains summary descriptions of the five (5) project components considered
under this EA.
These are:
* Akamiah Sewage Project
* Safiyah East Sewage Project
* Saba'een Sewage Project
* Sawad Hanash Sewage Project
* Hasabah Water Supply
In total some 395 hectares of the city is proposed to be sewered under the Long-term
Program, and some 325 hectares connected to the water supply network. The areas concerned
were located on Figure 1.1.
The following sections 3.2 and 3.3 provide the rationale for the projects, their need, objectives
and anticipated benefits. This is provided separately for the Sanitation and Water Supply
projects.
Sections 3.4 to 3.8 describe the projects individually. These sections include a brief outline of
physical project components and processes and the relationship between project components
and elements of existing systems on which they may be dependent.
The detail provided below in the project descriptions varies considerably and reflects the
extent of project development thus far.
3.2        SANITATION-PROJECTS

The selected project areas have been identified for priority works aimed at relieving serious
sewage related problems. The areas, shown on Figure 3.1, have variable characteristics:

Y9701                                                                     - 3.2 -                                                          March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
All sites are connected to the water supply system (albeit receiving supplies only periodically),
but existing public health and environmental conditions are extremely poor, with raw sewage
frequently running along streets and ponding at low points. In some of the more extreme cases
sewage enters private houses and mosques.
The source of the problem is the percolation pits, normally located in the middle of the street,
to which each building is conmected. These rarely function properly as a result of the clogging
of soil pores and a past lack of cleaning and now overflow on a regular, if not continuous
basis. Vacuum trucks may be used to withdraw some of the septic sewage, but this is
expensive and is an option rarely exercised. In addition, lack of regulation and enforcement
results in unacceptable disposal practices by the operators, which simply transfer the problem
elsewhere.
When asked to rank their concerns over sanitation respondents the 520 households involved in
the ESS surveys (carried out in the Akamah and neighbouring Sawad Hanash Areas) clearly
identified three major sanitation issues:
Street flooding (with) Sewage:         45% of first choice
- Cost incurred in cleaning cesspits:  30% of first choices
High incidence of water related disease:    52% of second choices
The proposed projects are intended to alleviate these problems by:
-     demolishing and making safe all of the percolation pits; and
-     the construction of a sewerage system connecting the area to the existing public
network.
3.2.2     DesiLn Criteria
(i)    General
Attempts were made to identify national specifications or standards related to pipes, fittings,
valves and accessories and discussions on the matter were held with offices of the Yemen
Organisation for Standardisation, Meteorology and Quality Control.
It is apparent that national standards covering these areas have not yet been developed.
International standards, BS and 10-States, have therefore been adopted as appropriate.
The design criteria for the project (and for all other Sewage related project components unless
otherwise specified) are as outlined in Table 3.1.
(ii)   Pipe Material
Pipe materials were selected on the basis of a number of criteria:
* Resistance against chemical attack, intemally from the sewage and externally from the soil
conditions.
* Smoothness of inner wall surface.
Y9701                                   .3.3-                              March 1999



Sana a Water Supply and Sanitation Services                Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
* Mechanical strength and resistance to backfill and traffic load with least additional
protection.
* Local availability; Regionally or locally manufactured or available in the local market or,
neighbouring countries.
* Ease of use; Pipe weight, range of length available as standard.
* Cost.

(iii) Manholes
Manholes will be provided for inspection and maintenance at junctions, drops, changes in pipe
diameters, directions and gradient, and at regular intervals to facilitate cleaning. They will be
either pre-cast circular rings or cast in situ for large sewers. Inner walls and benching will be
protected with tar epoxy lining.
Drop manholes will be provided for sewer diameters equal or smaller than 600 mm where the
difference in inverts of the inlet and outlet pipes exceeds 0.6 m. For larger diameter sewers,
drop manholes will be determined by pipe and fitting dimensions. Table 3.2 presents the type,
dimensions and spacing of manholes.
Table 3.2: Manholes Types. Dimensions and Spacing

Rectangular 1500 x 1500 mm (cast in-situ)   700 - 900                  80
3.3       SANITATION PROJECT DESCRIPTIONS
3.3.1     Akamah Seweraue Network Proiect: Sana'a 1
The Akamah Project area covers some 145 hectares and will house around 34,000 people at a
relatively high residential density, (in the context of Sana'a) of around 280 pph. It typically
comprises low quality housing developments but also contains two main commercial streets.
The network will serve a population of about 41,000 inhabitants by 2005 and thereafter. The
basic network layout is shown on Figure 3.2.
(i)    Project Components
The proposed project comprises two contract components:
(a)     Area Collection Network
This comprises a gravity sewer network for an area of approximately 145 ha. The system is
designed as a separate system, i.e. for sewerage only, not a combined storm drainage and
sewerage system. Some 1,060 house connections will be provided via 150 mm uPVC pipes
running from private inspection chambers to a collection chamber then to the nearest manhole
or sewer line. A house connection comprises 3 connections to individual inspection chambers
as per Figure 3.3, therefore about 3,180 households will be added to the sewer network.
The proposed collection network comprises:
-     28,000m of 200-400mm pipes,
-     2,150m of 500 - 600mm uPVC pipes
-     820 manholes.
-     1,060 house connections

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services          Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
According to the SWSS, some 88% of households are now connected to the NWSA network
and receive water on average once every 4.5 to 5.0 days.
By these parameters the area is relatively well served. However these figures disguise the true
situation. The distribution system comprises ad hoc networks of small diameter pipes
operating at very low pressures. Thus while water may be available it is only in very limited
quantities. This is reflected most clearly in the relative expenditure by residents of Hasabah on
tankered water estimated in the SWSS survey, Table 3.7.
Table 3.7: Cost of Water for the Families that Collected Water from Other Sources (YR)
Area                  By Water Tankers (Over By Bottled Water Bought
the Last Month)      (During 1 Day)
Safiyah                      1,392                34.69
Hasabah                      1,450                44.12
Industrial complex           1,286                23.33
Saba'een                     1,217                 60
Average: Project Areas       1,347                 39.3
Survey Area Average          1,238                39.46
Moreover hydraulic analysis undertaken in the preparation of the Development Plan indicates
that the existing network could not provide minimum water requirements2 to much of the area
even if water supplies were to be available in significantly increased quantities.
The area has recently been sewered in a project funded by NWSA. Under present conditions
this system will not function efficiently. There will be insufficient flow.
Design Parameters
The Hasabah area is one of the more densely populated areas in Sana'a but is projected to
have a density in the year 2000 of only 147 people per hectare. The ultimate density of 200
people per hectare is projected to be reached by year 2005. No further population infill is to be
accommodated. The total population to be served is therefore estimated at 65,000.
A supply provision equivalent to a domestic consumption rate of B0 I/c/d is targeted for this
area in the Sector Development Plan (SDP). Community (non-domestic) consumption is set at
33 percent of total consumption, equivalent to 40 I/cld in this case. Accordingly, total
domestic and community water consumption is projected to reach 120 l/c/d by the year 2020.
Available records of unaccounted for water (the margin between water produced and water
sold) were collected from NWSA, Sana'a Branch. These are listed below, as a percentage of
water consumed (produced):
Year                       1992     1993     1994     1995      1996     1
Unaccounted for water    | 41.2%   | 33.3%    34.5%    32.0%/ | 33.3%
2 The minimum water requirement is defined at 35 l/cld by the draft Cabinet Resolution on Water Sector
Reform and adopted in the Development Programme as the minimum option
Y9701                                    -3.17-                             March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana a I Environmental Impact Assessment
These system losses are expected to decrease in the future following the rehabilitation of the
distribution network and as a result of the anticipated leak detection programmes and related
measures proposed in the SDP. The target maximum loss is set at 20% in the SDP.
Therefore, with a basic consumption rate of 801Vc/d, a 33% allowance for non domestic
consumption and a further 20% for losses, the design per capita consumption rate is 150 l/c/d.
On this basis, total water consumption in Hasabah is expected to be around 3.2 MCMly.
Design Criteria
(i)    General
Attempts were made to identify national specifications or standards related to pipes, fittings,
valves and accessories and discussions on the matter were held with offices of the Yemen
Organisation for Standardisation, Meteorology and Quality Control.
It was determined that national standards covering these areas have not yet been developed.
International standards (BS) have therefore been adopted as appropriate.
The design criteria for the project are as outlined in Table 3.8.
(ii)   Pipe Material
The following materials assessed for suitability for use under the projected physical and
hydraulic conditions of the Hasabah Water Supply Project and found satisfactory: Ductile Iron
(D.I.), uPVC, and for small diameter pipes Polyethylene (PE) and Galvanised Mild Steel
(G.M.S.).
Further assessment based on the following criteria led to the selection of Ductile Iron (D.I.)
Class K9 for pipe diameters greater than 400 mm and uPVC Class PNIO for pipe diameters 80
mm to 400 mm.
x Mechanical strength and resistance to backfill and traffic load with least additional
protection.
* Local availability; Regionally or locally manufactured or available in the local market or,
neighbouring countries.
* Ease of use; Pipe weight, range of length available as standard.
* Cost (Table 3.9).
The working pressure of uPVC pipes was selected as 10 bars to allow for decrease in pipe
pressure ratings caused by water temperature. This class of pipes will withstand the allowed
maximum pressure in the network (65 m) at temperatures higher than 35 C. The measured
maximum water temperature in Sana'a public network is around 30 *C.

(iii) Fire Fighting System
The water distribution system will be designed as a combined network to cater for the
requirement of the Civil Defence in Sana'a City and the international code of practice for fire
fighting (NFPA). The fire fighting tankers (Pumpers) of the Civil Defence will obtain the water
for fire fighting from fire hydrants acting as filling points. The minimum pressure of 20.0 m
adopted at each node of the network is sufficient to fill these tankers.
The Hasabah area is considered a low risk area for the following reasons:
D Most buildings are one or two floor buildings and are not interconnected. Typically
buildings will be separated by at least Im of open space, usually considerably more.
* Building materials used are mainly stone and concrete (86% of all buildings in 1994), which
are fire resistant.
These conditions will lower the risk of fire spread and the occurrence of large fires.
Accordingly, the total fire fighting demand is calculated based on the assumption of the
occurrence of two fire events, each fire will be fought off by two fire streams for a duration of
two hours. The required water will be stored in the water storage reservoirs of the distribution
network.
Fire hydrants will be instaled on pipe diameters not less than 150 mm in order not to cause
excessive pressure losses in the water supply system.
3.4.2     Proiect Components
The Hasabah area falls within the low level pressure zone of the Sana'a water supply system.
The primary network designed in the Development Programme for the low zone is retained in
this study and the Hasabah secondary distribution network was designed accordingly.
All house connection components supply and the installation of underground works are
included under the Project scope of work. However, above ground house connections works
are excluded. These are to be carried out by NWSA.
Y9701                                   -3.20 -                           March 1999

Project works (Figure 3.8) will comprise the following:
* Excavate trenches not exceeding < 2 m depth and lay:
-  21,700 m of pipes of diameter 200 mm and less
-  2,700 m of pipes of diameter 400 and 500 mm
-  2,700 m of pipes of 800'mm diameter.
* Install various valves and accessories (see Table 3.8)
* Install valve chambers as required (approximately 25)
* Install fire hydrants (approximately 80)
* Flushing and disinfection of laid pipes
* Restoration of existing surfaces
According to NWSA Sana'a Branch, most of the existing water pipes constructed in Phase
One are either damaged or leaking and therefore they will be replaced. The pipes constructed
during Phase Two are about 15 years old and made of D.I. pipes. Most of these pipes are in
good physical condition and may be relied on for the next 25 years. Accordingly, some 7,000
metres of existing network pipes will be retained in the proposed water supply network.

4.        BASELINE STUDIES
4.1       INTRODUCTION
This Section outlines the physical and socio-economic context for the project. Section 4.2
deals with the description of the physical environment and natural resources of the study area,
and their status. Section 4.3 provides some background data on the affected populations, size,
age, sex structure and level of education. This section also includes a brief assessment of
gender issues.
Income and expenditure pattems are reviewed briefly in Section 4.4. As with most such
reviews this is relatively brief and while it the individual data sources utilised provide for a
relatively coherent pattern internally, there are apparent discrepancies between sources.
Section 4.5 contains a description of the urban environments in which the project is set; the
urban planning framework and rates of urban growth, housing conditions, service provision,
etc. Section 4.6 deals with the specific issue of public health.
The final Section 4.7 provides a brief overview of the quality of the available data base and
highlights areas of potential significant weakness for the definition of potential impacts.
Throughout this Section data is provided at one of three levels; National, Sana'a urban area,
and Project sites as it is available.
4.2       PHYSICAL CONTEXT
4.2.1     Soils and Structural Geolo2v
Sana'a city is situated on a plain of Quaternary alluvium surrounded by mountains, mainly of
Quaternary and Tertiary Volcanics. The alluvial deposits consist of horizons of clay, loess like
silt, sand, gravel and larger particles and contain appreciable volcanic detritus. The deposits
become finer grained to the north.
The Tertiary Volcanics can be distinguished into three layers: Basalt, chaotic volcanics and
chaotic and stratoid volcanics.  The black, sometimes green-black basalt, is a dense
homogeneous basalt flow with columnar jointing. The chaotic volcanics consist of a non-
homogeneous mixture of volcanic products and alluvial deposits. The chaotic and stratoid
volcanics include more homogeneous stratoid rocks such as tuff.
The Tawilah Sandstone, a medium to fine grained fluviatile and deltaic sandstone and
conglomerate, occasionally interbedded with mudstone and siltstone layers, underlies much of
the Plain to the south of the city at depth, and outcrops to north west and north east.
While, at the regional level, the structural geology is reasonably complex, with extensive fault
systems and associated structures -present, the Sana'a basin itself is typically tensive, with the
vertical movements on the plateau minor, especially when compared to those of the
escarpment facing the Red Sea. Nevertheless, as would be expected with its proximity to the
Red Sea Rift Fault system the study area is moderately seismically active.
Y9701                                  - 4.1 -                           March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services          Sana aa I Environmental Impact Assessment
Soil investigations have been undertaken for the Akamah and Hasabah Areas. These were
carried out in May 1998 as indicated in Table 4. 1.
Table 4.1  Site Investigation Programme
Area                       No of Pits                  Depth of Pit
Akamiah                        14                  5.5m below ground surface
Hasabah                        12                           3m
In both cases, Standard Penetration Tests (SPT) were performned in the pits at 2.Om depth
(intervals) from the surface and bulk samples were collected for laboratory testing.
The laboratory tests included sieve analysis and determination of the Atterberg limits for the
material passing No. 4 Sieve.
The overall (standard) profile defined is as below:
Akamah                                Hasabah
Fill layer    0 - 25cm    Sand, Silt, clay, plastic  0 - 40cm  Sand, Silt, clay, plastic bags,
bags, paper, etc.                    paper, etc.
Alluvial     25 - 130cm   Range: Fine silty clay to  40 -1 10cm    Range: Fine silty clay to
Material                 coarse gravelly sand.                coarse gravelly sand.
Bedrock     11 pits (none)                       11 pits (none)
_      3 pits    Basalt                      1 pit    IBrownish weathered siltstone
The fill layer comprises a mixture of sand, silt, clay and gravel and also contains various waste
materials, pieces of paper and cloth, polythene bags, etc. Given that the fill material will have
to be removed and not reutilised in excavation works, it was not analysed further.
The alluvial deposits encountered were common to both sites. They typically comprised a
variety of soil mixtures ranging from fine silty clay to coarse gravely sand with the presence of
boulders and cobbles on some occasions. The variability of the materials encountered is typical
of alluvium, where the particle size of the soil deposits depends on their mode of deposition
and is greatly influenced by the carrying stream shape and gradient.
In both areas, the reported SPT results were highly variable. At Hasabah, the average SPT
value N was 27 with a Standard Deviation of 15 and at Akamnah the N value was 19 but with a
Standard Deviation of 13. In general, these values correlate to loose to medium dense
cohesionless soils with an angle of internal friction, + of 28 to 30 degrees. Where cohesive
soils were encountered (Akamah), the recorded N values were of stiff to very stiff clays of an
unconfined compressive strength of 1 to 4 kg/cmr. No clear relationship could be deduced
between N values and depth of test.
Groundwater was not encountered at any of the pits.
Excavation in the alluvial deposits will be relatively easy and will not warrant the use of heavy
excavating equipment such as rippers or jack hammers. The clayey materials found are stiff
enough to stand sub-vertical, without support, provided they are kept dry. However while the
cohesionless soils will also be stable with minimum slopes the variation in their relative density
and level of cementation is such that precautionary measures should be-undertaken. However,
side stability can be secured with nominal support.
Y9701                                    -4.2-                              March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitatton Services       Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
The cohesionless alluvial deposits are suitable for use as backfill, after removal of the
oversized particles (exceeding 100 mm). The finer cohesive materials may also be utilised
provided they do not contain material with a plasticity index exceeding 6 percent.
The basalt is black, moderately weathered, strong to very strong. Excavation in this rock will
require the use of mechanical rippers. Protection against rock spalling shall also be required
during excavation, especially where the rock is highly weathered. The siltstone belongs to the
Tawilah Sandstone formation and actually outcrops on the surface in the vicinity of the pit.
4.2.2     Topoeraphv
The study areas are almost entirely built up and have a generally low relief. Detailed (1:1000)
topographic mapping (streets only) available for Akamah and Hasabah provides a similar
picture. At Hasabah, the maximum and minimum height (masl) vary by around 20m with
gradients usually <1% occasionally increasing to 1.5% trending broadly SW to NE. Gradients
are slightly more pronounced in Akamah (change in elevation of 27m) but are still <2%, at 1
to 1.5%. Overall the dominant, SW-NE slope trend is evident though in this particular area
localised stronger west to east trends are present.
Elsewhere, the available mapping does not allow for any assessment although the pattern
evident for Hasabah and Akamah would be expected to be repeated.
4.2.3     Climate
In 1992, the Technical Secretariat of the High Water Council (TSHWC) defined a series of
climatic zones for the Northern Part of Yemen, under which the study area is variously
described as being in the Temperate Highland Zone and the intermontane plain depending on
the nature of the classification. Rainfall is generated by three meteorological phenomena:
* The Mediterranean Effect
In the winter months, (especially in December and January) the influx of polar air that follows
the passage of a depression may trigger light rainfall. However, such events are infrequent
occurring on average every 3-5 winter seasons.
* The Red Sea Convergence Zone (RSCZ)
The RSCZ is caused by the rapid heating up of land surfaces (relative to sea warming) that
gives rise to the generation of onshore winds that are then affected by the convection effects
of the Western Highlands. As a consequence humid air masses are lifted and carried eastward
to give rise to heavy, but short and highly localised, rainstorms. The RSCZ is at its most
influential from March to May, and to less notable effect in the autumn.
* Monsoonal ITCZ
The Monsoonal ITCZ influences Yemen from July to September when warm dry air from the
Arabian (and African) land masses to the North mixes with moist southerlies from the Indian
Y9701                                 -4.3-                             March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services       Sana a I Environmental Impact Assessment
Ocean. The rain producing effects of the ITCZ are most keenly felt on the Western and
Southern Escarpments where the triggering orographic effects are most significant. The
Eastern Escarpment receives relatively little in the way of rain from the influence of the ITCZ,
though as the ITCZ moves north (before retreating south again) areas as far east and north as
the Sana'a plain may receive rainfall benefits.
Micro relief effects are significant in the Sana'a Basin which has higher land both to the west
and east.
Mean maximum monthly temperatures on the Central Highlands, while in the temperate range,
are still moderately high at 23.5 - 29.6 'C. There is however a marked diurnal range at higher
elevations often over 150 in the summer and more 18 -20 IC in winter. This pattern is reflected
on an annual basis with significant variation in the winter and summer minimums and
maximums. In winter, the absolute minimum is -6.5 'C with mean monthly minimum of
0.5 IC. The summer absolute maximum in the collected records, is 31.5 'C, however,
temperatures were recorded in summer 1998.
Evaporation rates are high totalling in excess of 2000 mm throughout the Sana'a Plain-though
again the influences of altitude and microrelief on a range of climatic data, Humidity,
Radiation, and Evapotranspiration Data are clearly evident.
Overall moisture deficits incurred in the Central Highlands are in the range 1600 - 1700 mm
per annum.
Principal climatic data for three stations in Sana'a are provided in Figure 4.1.
4.2.4     Hvdrologv
The principal surface hydrological feature in the study area is Wadi Sailah, which runs north -
south through the city.
In the past the wadi has had a multiple function, as a drainage channel, as a green space, and
as7a funnel channelling the dominant north easterly and northerly winds through the city. These
functions are now severely degraded. The findings of the Sana'a Urban ecology survey of
1995 summarised in Table 4.2 remain valid today with in most cases some further
deterioration in conditions.
Unless corrective measures are taken it would appear inevitable that the wadi will be continue
to be urbanised and developed with potentially very severe adverse affects.
From the specific hydrological perspective the present carrying capacity of the channel with
respect 10, 25, 50 or 100 year return floods is unknown. While project areas themselves may
not be directly affected by the Wadi the main sewer Interceptors taidng sewage from the city
to the Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP) to the north of the city use the wadi as a right of
way.

No air quality surveys have been undertaken for this study and meetings held with the EPC
confirm they have yet to initiate an air quality monitoring programme in Sana'a. A monitoring
station is now available at the EPC Headquarters and is expected to be operational shortly. It
is planned, for the longer term, to establish a network of stations throughout the cify.

4.2.6      Noise
Discussions with the EPC have indicated that:
- no noise monitoring has been undertaken, and
*  it is the qualitative / judgmental assessment of EPC staff that noise is not a significant
concern within Yemeni culture. Indeed, the concept of noise pollution is not readily
recognised and the potential adverse impacts on health etc. are not generally understood.
Moreover, despite the age of much of the vehicle fleet and driver characteristics, the relatively
low levels of traffic; and the infrequency of flight operations from Sana'a airport dictate that
the principal sources of noise concern in most cities are muted in their effect in Sana'a.
Similarly while there are a large number of individual point sources, factories, etc. these too
are generally not a major source of concern. The most significant, in a localised context are the
motors driving private water pumps.

in the range of 150 - 300 for mature neighbourhoods and only 84 pph for the approved zones
as a-whole. Only in one exceptional case did gross densities exceed 350 persons per hectare.

4.3.4      Ponulation Characteristics
(i)    Household Size
Household sizes in Sana'a are relatively high when compared to the National average. They
are also high in comparison with other Middle Eastern countries. For example, they are well
above those of Jordan as shown in Table 4.7. Moreover, household size in Yemen appears
also to have increased in comparison with the Census of 1984, when they were estimated at
6.32 persons, compared with 6.74 in 1994.
Table 4.7 Average Household Size: Yemen and Jordan: 1994
Jordan      _      Aveae Hhold Size    Yemen                 Average Hhold Size
Madaba Rural            6.60          Sana'a Govemorate            7.41
Zarqa                   6.28
Amman                   5.79          Sana'a City                  6.79
National                6.16          National                     6.74
Source: Jordan, General Census of Population and Housing 1994, Yemen 1994 Census.
In the future, household sizes will be expected to decline broadly in line with the decline in
rates of natural population growth. However, even by the end of the plan period household
sizes will remain relatively large.
In the context of the water and sanitation sector these figures imply that the basic unit of
supply, will also remain large facilitating the development of an effective delivery system with
relatively few connections. To place this in perspective a reduction in household size from the
present Sana'a City level of 6.79 to that for Zarqa (6.28) and Amman (5.79) will reduce the
number of domestic units by 33,500 and 71,772, equivalent to some 54% and 110% of 1997
estimated household connections respectively.

(ii)   Population Structure
Analysis of the population structure of Sana'a shows that it reflects underlying trends typical
of the Region, in particular having a high ratio of young population. With 40 % to 45 %
under the age of 15 and over 50 % under 19, the values for Yemen are at the high end of
expectations. (See Table 4.8).
Table 4.8: Simplified Age / Sex Structure of Sana'a City Population (%f°)

In the short term the implications of this structure will be felt in:
* considerable pressure for the creation of jobs, with an annual entry into the Sana'a job
market of 10 000 - 15 000 persons per annum2,
* the creation of major stresses on public services, in particular the education system which
would, under conditions of universal primary education, expect 25 000 to 30 000 entrants
to the system annually to the end of the project period.
* substantial increase in the female population in reproductive cohorts.
(iii)   Households Per Structure
As the data from the SWSS provided in Tables 4.9 and 4.10 overleaf suggest, the average
number of households per structure is closer to two than one. More surprisingly the number of
households per floor appears to be relatively constant irrespective of the overall number of
floors of the building.
This is reflected in the consistently high room occupancy rates. A figure of 2.2 persons per
room is reported for Akamah and Hasabah.
4.3.5     Education
Education levels are generally low with national adult illiteracy rates among the highest in the
Region and well above the MENA average. The position is not as extreme in Sana'a. Even so,
over 40% of adult women and around 20%/ of adult males are illiterate, Table 4.11.

4.3.6      Gender Issues
At a general level and highly simplistic level, Yemeni society draws clear distinctions between
the responsibilities of men and women:
Women: Are the dominant force in the home, and within the house. They are traditionally
responsible for cleaning the house, preparing the food and educating children. Some of the
work, washing, (cleaning, helping prepare food) may be delegated to the children, primnarily
daughters. Women may be also be restricted in their movement, being allowed to leave the
house only in the afternoon and then only to visit relatives or female friends.
Men: Are traditionally expected to represent the family outside the house. He is responsible
for obtaining the family income and usually will do the shopping. In circumstances, where
some conflict in roles may be perceived to apply, such as carrying solid waste to rubbish
containers or collecting water, children are usually delegated the task. Where no conflict is felt
to exist, women will be responsible.
The right of women to work and participate in the development process without hindrance or
discrimination was established in legislation3 in the constitutions of the YAR and the former
PDRY and since unification in the Constitution of the ROY. Despite this it is clear from the
available data (Table 4.12) that women have either been unable, or have not desired, (or both)
3 YAR clause 36: "Every citizen has the right to practice the career he or she chooses within the limits of
the law"
clause 10     "Yemeni citizens are equal in the right of labor"
clause 34     All texts organizing the employment of both sexes are appLicable to working women
without distinction ......
PDRY clause 27: "Labor is the right and duty of every capable citizen, corresponding with his or her
ability, qualification and rights without distinction based on age, sex, blood, color,
religion or language"
ROY           Labor is a right and honor: it is deemed inevitable in developing the society. Every citizen
has the right to practice the job he chooses for himself within the limits of the law. It is
unlawful to impose through compulsory means jobs on any citizen, except as stated by the
law to carry out a public service without fair wages in return
Y9701                                      -4.13 -                                 March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services              Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
to participate fully in the workplace and that there has been little or no change in the last 10
years.
Table 4.12:   Economic Participation Rates %: (10 years and over)

Males           51.64       54.46       54.68       54.37

Factors that are still perceived to restrict this participation include:
*  Society perceives woman's primary role as in the home to the extent that that is where the
woman will inevitable end up. Accordingly, education is not a necessity for women, many
of whom may suffer restricted access to basic education. This is strongly reflected in the
variable literacy rates for men and women (Table 4.12 above) and in particular, at later
stages, in access to a scientific and technical education. Laws requiring compulsory
universal primary education have been in existence of laws for many years they are rarely if
ever enforced and in some cases especially in rural areas no girl only facilities are available
because of a shortage of female teachers.
* Even when educated initially, strongly perceived pre-eminence of role in the home
associated with very young first marriage and very high total fertility rates combine (Table
4.13) to reduce secondary and further education opportunities and limit female skill
development opportunities.
* Conflict with the traditional view that the man is the income earner, exacerbated by both,
the potential social stigma that may be attached to a man whose wife is either the sole or
part income, and the potential competition for some between men and women jobs.

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
Surveys undertaken in 1985, 1991 and more recently in 1994 do indicate positive trends
suggesting for example that the overwhelming majority of urban Heads of Household approve
of women being allowed to work.
In addition, to the general constraints imposed on participation in-the labour market, women
also appear to be disadvantaged in that where they do participate they are not fully utilised. In
general terms women tend to be employed in the public sector, often in 'reserved occupations'
where they have little opportunity to develop a professional career and or where their potential
is rarely fully realised.
Women have been successful in the broader development process only in those areas that are
culturally allocated to them, maternity care, child nutrition, illiteracy programmes etc.
Nevertheless, the successes in these areas, and especially those promoted and supported by the
General Union of Yemeni Women should not be underestimated or undervalued.
In project specific terms the following conclusions are drawn:
* Given that no land acquisition and resettlement is proposed, there will be no circumstances
in which women are deprived of their due compensation rights.
• There will be significant loss of employment and as such no particular disadvantage to
women through employment loss.
* Similarly, women are unlikely to benefit directly from reductions in workload. These
benefits are most likely to fall to children who are charged with carrying out most domestic
duties outside of the home; fetching water, disposing of waste, etc.
The primary area of benefit will be in the reduction of time and other resources spent in crisis
management as the family manager; fewer occurrences of disease, fewer sick children, reduced
health costs and greater productive time with children.

Data on household and per capita income in Sana'a City and for Yemen as a whole are scarce.

(of 6.79 persons) per month, equivalent to US$170-177 per capita/year at current (October
1997) exchange rates.

There are however considerable differences in the results of the SWSS and the ESS, especially
in two areas:
(i)    The indirect costs/expenditure incurred in supplementing either the quantity or quality
of water available
The ESS identify a number of additional expenditures by households as follows
-     12% of households filter tap water because of fears of contamination; estimated cost
$0.13 per capita.
-     Estimated time spent fetching water; 11 minutes a day for an average 2.5 I/c
equivalent. Not costed.
-     More than 30% of households purchase water at a cost of $10.95 per capita per
annum. This compares to 12% in SWSS survey areas and range of from <10% to 25%.
-     Overall expenditures on all types of water estimated at $30per capita per annum, say
$200 per household. This compares with the SWSS results that put the unweighted
average at $158 and the absolute maximum, at $174 per household.
(ii)   Health costs
Health costs reported in the ESS are estimated at $16 per capita or around $112 per family
some 5% of total family income. These compare with estimates of $1.85 per capita, or around
0.6% of family income.
In both cases, it is important to stress, that the issue is one of the scale of the costs incurred,
and not whether they are incurred.

4.5       URBAN DEVELOPMENT
4.5.1     Urban Planning Framework
Urban Planning is the responsibility of the Ministry of Construction, Housing and Urban
Planning (MCHUP) and as noted in Section 3, an Urban Planning Law has been introduced
and provides for three levels of planning to take place, Regional, Master and Detailed. Thus,
while past experience of the execution of urban planning programmes has not been particularly
encouraging, the fact remains that the legislative and administrative structure is in place.
At present, no comprehensive programme of Master Planning or detailed planning is ongoing
and there appears to be no intent to re-start the GTZ and MoCHUP "Planning Assistance for
Urban Development" programme of the mid-1990s. That programme completed much of the
basic groundwork required to undertake both Master and Detailed Planning, including the
preparation of design guidelines and standards, and most of that work will still remain valid.
However, delays of more than two to three more years in re-establishing the programme or an
alternative, will require most of it to be repeated.
4.5.2     Urban Growth Pattern
As Table 4.17 shows, since 1967, urban growth in Sana'a has been explosive, expanding from
a smaUl settlement of some 6 - 700 hectares and accommodating 56000 people to the present
city accommodating nearly 1 million people and sprawling over some 11000 hectares of
approved development areas.
Table 4.17: Urban Growth: Sana'a 1967 - 1997
Urbanised Area    Annual Growth Rate      Annual
Year              (km,)             (%/6)          (km eiuiv.perannum)
1967              7.0
1979              20.9              9.5                    1.2
1985              44.6              13.5                   4.0
1990              77.4              11.6                   6.6
1995              94.5              5.1                    4.3
Source: Sana'a Urban Ecology, 1995; Italconsult 1975
In 1967 the urban area was largely confned to four areas:
-     two central high density zones; the western area of the Old City including the Souk and
a second around Gaa Al-Ulufy.
-     a third central zone, around Gaa Al-Ulufy of limited density (<85pph)
-     new developments to the south and the north of the Old Town
-     the remaining part of the urban area characterised by very low densities.
By 1979, the developed areas were Rawdhah and the airport, and the Sana'a city. The
principle growth axis at this time was to the north, a process that was to be reinforced by the
recommendations of the 1978 Louis Berger/ Kampsax Plan that from a purely physical
perspective made complete sense, i.e. the mountains to the east and west should constrain
growth of the urban corridor to a predominantly north - south, axis.
Y9701                                 -4.18-                            Marth 1999



Sana'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services        Sana'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
However, while the basic alignment of growth has been as intended, the greater majority of the
development has occurred to the south, and on the primary road corridor to Taiz (SE), and to
a lesser extent along the following corridors:
* to the west along the Hodeidah road
* to the north-west along the Wadi Dahr road
This is in direct contrast to the specific intent of the 1978 Master Plan, where it is
recommended that expansion to the south of the city be curtailed.
4.5.3     Housina and Housing Conditions
The housing stock of Sana'a is characterised by the following:
-     Generally large houses or villas built on a single plot of land often with a garden. These
will typically be of one to three storeys (more usually one and two) and may
accommodate more than one household. Usually all households on one plot will be
from the same extended family unit.
-     Apartments; rarely of more than seven storeys in height.
-     Ushah/Sandakha; temporary or impermanent structures more often than not made from
a combination of materials, wood, zinc, hay and plastic sheet.
-     Others may include emergency housing, work housing or collective housing
(dormitories, prisons etc.)
Overall, bearing in mind the relatively low estimated per capita income the quality of the
housing stock in Sana'a city is good. There are only a few obvious examples of concentrations
of low-quality housing. However where these do occur, they tend to be located on lands
previously considered undevelopable and have their origins in a specific local or regional
event. As such the communities involved tend to be somewhat more homogenous than is
found elsewhere in the city.
The most obvious examples are the squatter settlements along the banks and increasingly
within Wadi Sailah. Aside from being unsightly and unsanitary these camps are under serious
(and as the % of hard areas in the catchment increases, increasingly serious) threat from flash
floods
Outside of these areas, and the confines of the Old City, residential developments in Sana'a are
unusually heterogeneous incorporating a wide range of land uses, building types and building
quality. Perhaps more than anything else this steams from two factors:
-     the explosive growth of the city and the its relative structural immaturity
-     social structures and customs in Yemen that place considerable emphasis on activities
and events within the property but place little emphasis on the exterior:
The most significant value is the very low level of service provision, estimated in this Table at
what is considered by the Consultants to be a very generous 25%.
Y9701                                 -4.19 -                            March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services           Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
4.5.4      Service Provision
In terms of service provision, the picture is less satisfactory. While, as Table 4.18 indicates the
proportion of households supplied with water from the public system is relatively high at 88%6
this figure disguises the inadequacy of the supply provided, described at length elsewhere. The
values for the power system are considerably better and reflect much more closely the true
level of service provision.
Table 4.18: Percentage of Dwellings Supplied by Public Service Systems
