All areas to be sewered in the Sanitation project are served by the Public supply network
though supply provision is intermittent (usually a minimum of one day in four, but often
better) and variable between areas depending on location. The Hasabah area has recently been
sewered.
4.5.5      Physical Environment
Until the mid 1960s Sana'a was a largely traditional urban centre that had developed an urban
ecology that was largely in balance. The population estimated at around 50 000 had grown
only very slowly if at all over preceding centuries. The consumption patterns of the economic
structure (of which the population is an integral part) based on traditional technologies in
construction, transport, water supply energy provision etc. were by necessity balanced with
available regional resources and would also include significant recycling.
However, the radical political change of the mid-1960s and the period of massive population
growth and associated economic and social change that followed and persists to the present
day have all but destroyed that ecological balance.
Today, the quality of the urban environment of Sana'a is poor and under pressure from a range
of factors is continuing to deteriorate. Of principal concern are:
* Solid Waste Dumps (Dumping)
Most parts of the city are scattered with what are effectively solid waste dumpsites. Typically
such sites will result from the overfilling of containers and use of common land in particular
wadi beds (Wadi Sailah for example) as open dumps, The factors contributing to the problem
are numerous and the interrelationships between them are complex. However, Schluetter 1995
identifies four principal factors:
'6 The data contained in this Table is restricted to that in the 1996 Statistical Yearbook. It is not compared
and assessed against the data obtained from other sources that may conflict with its findings
Y9701                                    -4.20-                               March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
(i)    Generally poor sector management; from wrong location of containers, poor choice of
containers, inadequate maintenance of vehicles, etc.
(ii)   Lack of awareness among residents and cultural issues. Islamic tradition and teachings
promotes hygiene and the responsible use of natural resources and these are evident in
the cleanliness of individual homes and some public facilities such as mosques. The
major problems occur in public places, roads, wadi beds etc. where old traditional
management systems such as those based on the muhtasib or equivalent have been
replaced by central government or municipal agencies which clearly do not function
with the same degree of effectiveness or community support, (as reflected in the
present extremely lax attitude to disposal of waste).
(iii)   Role of urban livestock. Despite the entirely urban nature of much of life in Sana'a, a
number of herdsmen (usually women or children) persist in browsing urban areas
(mainly on the outskirts of the city and in the old town) with their sheep and goats to
replace the feedstuff of the common rangelands that have either been lost to urban
development or from which they are now excluded for one reason or another.
(iv)   Societal structure: A strong link between low quality housing areas and problematic
solid waste dumps
Sewage pools
Raw sewage pools occur throughout the city collecting in both natural depressions and man
made low points. The sewage originates from three sources:
(i)    Overflow: Percolation pit overflow is by far the most significant factor in this regard.
(ii)   Direct discharge: In specific locations the existing sewage network discharges into
open, usually natural, channels. In some cases infiltration / seepage (with resultant sub
surface and soil contamination) and natural grades will combine to prevent surface
lakes forming. Elsewhere where natural channel beds have been modified large and
small retention ponds have been created.
(iii)   Sewage System  Leakage: This is relatively minor, contributing much more
significantly to the pollution of groundwater resources.
The brief review of key factors above is not to say that other factors can be ignored. They
must not. Factors such as the Air pollution, from diesel pumps and generators, cars and
construction materials, Noise pollution, from diesel pumps and generators, cars, and Water
Wastage, all contribute to the production of a very heavily degraded urban environrent,
especially in public areas.

investigated.
* The incidence of Malaria reportedly virtually unknown on the Sana'a plain! 10 years ago is

and regional resources
* Hepatitis has now grown into a major public health threat to all members of the population.

4.6.2     SWSS
Data derived from the SWSS on the relationships between the incidence of diarrhoea,
intestinal infections and skin diseases, and the source of families' water supply or their method
of sewage disposal, are shown in Table 4.22.
The following tentative conclusions may be drawn from the data:
* 100 % of the sampled households in three of the areas of the City suffering most from the
specified diseases (Himmyar, Taiz Street and Safiyah) have connections to the NWSA
water supply network. Between 47% and 500% of households in these areas suffered from
the specified diseases, against an average for the whole sample of 39%/o.
* The fourth- area with 100% NWSA coverage (Musayek/Old City) had a relatively low
incidence of disease, but this could be explained by the fact that this area has the highest
' Child Survival Project

Sana a Water Supply and Sanitation Services           Sana a I Environmental Impact Assessment
single rate of connections to the NWSA sewage network (50%) of any of the sampled areas
(see (iii) below).
* The three sampled areas with a high proportion of water supplies from private networks
(Saba7een, Industnial Complex and Haddah) also suffered from an above average incidence
of water-borne diseases (between 44% and 49%). This again suggests that watel supplies
are contaminated, probably due in this case to seepage of sewage from cesspits into the
aquifers from which water supplies are drawn.
* There is no very obvious correlation between the use of welt/tanker water and water-bome
disease.
* The three sampled areas with the lowest incidence of water-borne or sewage-related
diseases (Hasabah, Musayek and Tahreer) all have relatively high rates of connection to the
NWSA sewerage network (between 20% and 50%). This suggests that the presence of
piped sewerage can contribute to a lower incidence of water-borne or sewage-related
sickness and, conversely, that the present system of cesspits (soakaways) on which 72% of
households depend for sewage disposal presents a serious health hazard.

4.6.3      Summary
While much of the above text has concentrated on the estimation of the actual costs of illness
related to poor sanitation these estimates provide little real information on the potential scale
of the public health problem in Sana'a and no indication of trends.
This can be derived only from the past published data which indicates that with the exception
of infant care and maternity the public health situation in Sana'a has not improved markedly
since 1975. On the contrary, when the increased prevalence of Malaria and Hepatitis and the
size of the population that may be at risk from a major epidemic are considert. it is not
unreasonable to conclude that the wider public health has declined.
Y9701                                   -4.24-                               March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
In the final analysis perhaps the most immediate measure of the potential threat to public
health of present sanitation conditions is provided by health professionals who in the
discussions undertaken for the ESS:
-     describe the incidence of water related diseases as above average,
-     suggest the conditions for a major outbreak  of cholera are met and that it would
spread like a fire in the forest.
4.7    DATA WEAKNESSES
The principal areas of data weakness encountered in preparing this report are as follows:
* Air quality and Noise
No monitoring data for the above was found. Any future assessment of impacts will therefore
have to be based one off stochastic assessments of baseline conditions
3 Income and expenditure
Although the SWSS and ESS provide some insight in to existing patterns of income and
expenditure in relation to the sector, there will be a need in the future to assess the benefits
and costs accruing to various sector development programmes much more precisely. In
particular the degree of impact on the poorer, disadvantaged sections of the community will
need to be more closely defined. To be able to do this using a consistent data set, potentially
over an extended period of time will require a far better data set than is at present available
from one off project related surveys.
* Public Health and Economic Costs and Benefits
The comment above on income and expenditure profiles applies in a wider context to the data
required to estimate the potential costs and benefits. Of perhaps most significance here is the
inability to relate medical record data to a place of residence and work / school attendance.
While there will always be extreme difficulty from a practical and theoretical perspective to
establish causal linkages between incidence of ill health and geographic areas, such data is
often of prime importance in quantifyting the potential benefits of a project.

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
5.        PROJECT IMPACTS
5.1       INTRODUCTION
This Section provides an overview of defined project impacts. These are assessed as:
Typical Impacts: i.e. impacts that may be attributable to a greater or lesser extent to both
project types. These are outlined in Section 5.2.
Sections 5.3 and 5.4 identify the impacts that are either related only to Sanitation or Water
Supply Projects or which are of particular significance in either context. In all cases impacts
are defined under one of four categories:
* Pre construction
* Construction Impacts; (temporary, off site and resource consumption)
* Permanent Impacts
* Operational Impacts
Induced, or indirect impacts are relatively minor and are considered under operational impacts.
The final section, 5.5 comprises a summary of impacts. In this context, while no differentiation
is made between sanitation project components in the text, the collection system or the trunk
main, clear distinctions are made in the summary tabulations.
5.2       TYPICAL IMPACTS
5.2.1     Pre Construction
There are only two aspects of project preparation to consider.
-    confirmation of the right to access lands for survey purposes, (topographic and subsoil),
-    subsoil investigation pits.
Neither is considered likely to be a source of major impacts, provided reasonable health and
safety measures are taken in the digging of the pits (which are not likely to exceed 6.5m)
5.2.2     Construction Works (Temnorarv)
Public Safety
During the construction phase, there may be an increased risk of accidents involving local
populations, especially children. These may result from one or a combination of the following:
-     unauthorised access to a construction site.
-     an absence of control over access to construction sites,
-     conflict with construction vehicles,
-     poor site safety,
-     inadequate site management.
r9701                                   -5.1-                              Marh 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services        Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
By their very nature construction activities generate elevated levels of accident risk. However,
three factors suggest that the impacts from these projects may be further increased:
* it is evident that a number of construction sites in Yemen lack proper management and in
some cases are clearly dangerous and equally importantly that off site activities, such as
construction traffic, are as poorly controlled.
* widespread construction within the urban area. Extensive linear construction sites along
urban streets will expose large sections of the population, especially children who use
streets as play areas, to risk.
* the nature of the urban development is such that access routes for construction traffic will
be poor; often comprising relatively narrow streets and lanes with no pedestrian facilities,
blind corners etc.
Noise and Construction Disturbance
During site preparation and construction, noise will be generated from a number of sources
including: jack hanmmers, excavators, payloaders, generators, etc. and in many instances these
will be operating in immediate proximity to residential areas.
Certain levels of noise disturbance are unavoidable. In excess, however, they can be a nuisance
to neighbouring residents and, in extreme cases, can become a health hazard to workers and
residents. Typically, operational noise will be broad-band but intermittent, and is in a wide
range from 5OdB(A) (non-operation) to 90+dB(A) as indicated in Table 5.1.
Table 5. 1: Tpical Noise Levels from Construction Equipment

From this it is clear that without exception night operations wil exceed standards and day
operations are uniformly excessive up to 20m. It is therefore inevitable, without mitigation,
that significant disturbance will be experienced by a substantial element of the population.
Air Quality
Unmanaged air pollution, especially of particulate and gaseous emissions from construction
machinery may create nuisance and in extreme cases direct adverse health impacts or damage
to property. However while some impacts can be expected throughout Project areas, the
quantity and nature of the machinery to be used is such that these impacts need not be severe.
Y9701                                  -5.2-                             March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitaion Sevices              Sana 'a I Enviromnental Impact Assessment
Construction traffic may potentially generate substantial quantities of dust in and around active
construction areas and on some of the unpaved access roads. This issue is linked to that of
heavy traffic use and access and safety on minor roads within the city.
It is also possible that off site facilities, in particular aggregate processing plants, may generate
sufficient dust to be of nuisance.
Waste Material
The alluviums in which the greater majority of the project works will be constructed will
contain horizons that are unsuited for use either as selected pipe bedding (contains material of
> 100 mm diameter) or backfill (plasticity index greater than 6%).
These materials will need to be carted away and disposed of appropriately and suitable
replacement materials brought in.
Hydrogeology
The groundwaters of the area may be affected by:
* Contamination of groundwater from minor spillage at contractor camps (septic tank and
leachate from trash and solid waste) or construction materials on site.
* Disturbances in soil physical properties may temporarily increase chances of seepages of
faecal material and other contaminants into groundwater.
* Increased potential mnixing of contaminated surface flows with those of the shallow aquifer/
water table
These imrpacts are however not expected to be significant. The construction sites will not be
deep enough to reach the groundwaters. Dewatering is therefore unlikely to be required and
mixing of waters precluded. The risk of release of hazardous materials' is minimal and the
additional load negligible in the context of that released by cesspits.
Fauna, Flora and Ecology
The wholly' urban context of the projects is likely to ensure that the threat of destruction of
habitats and associated displacement of fauna will be negligible. There are therefore
considered to be no direct impacts on the region's ecology from project works and almost
certainly no loss of biodiversity.
No hazardous materials are directly specified for use in the Bid Documents for the Sanitaton Projects.
However the water supply project will require flushing and disinfection of pipes using Calcium or Sodium
Hypochlorite or Chlorine, (see Section 5.4.2 . These materials, if spilt in bulk quantity, may have a very
short-term adverse impact on local groundwater quality, but no adverse consequences are anticipated.  r
2 There are no parks, green spaces or other possible refuge sites such as wadis that are directly affected by
Prject works.

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services            Sana ' I Environmental Impact Assessment
Archaeology/Cultural Heritage
The principal heritage and cultural values of the study area lie in occasional isolated examples
of traditional architecture. However, no sites of specific scientific, cultural or heritage
significance were identified that may be directly affected by Project works3. The rich cultural
and archaeological heritage of the old city is entirely unaffected by project works.
Moreover, virtually all project activities will occur within existing rights of way that have
already been subject to development. It is considered very unlikely therefore that any buried
site will be encountered in construction.
Off Site Works
Five types of off site facility may be developed to support the project:
(i)    Project offices
Project offices may located anywhere within Sana'a in any number of available existing
buildings and will generate no adverse impacts. Temporary, site offices may be established but
these will not be large and can be easily established and provided with necessary facilities at
little or no local impact.
(ii)    Materials Stockpiles
The Bid Documents indicate that the Client will provide a site for materials stockpiles. This
site is not yet identified, but may be at the client's headquarters site in Hasabah. The materials
for stockpile at this (or other defined) site will be exclusively non-hazardous and should not
require application of any exceptional handling and storage measures.
(iii)   Pre-Fabrication Yard
Elements for over 800 manholes will need to be pre cast off site. A yard will therefore be
required to store cement, chemicals (primarily oils) for the moulds, moulds and for pre
fabrication. It will require access to adequate water supplies and immediate access to suitable
(for heavy traffic) roads.
It is possible that these works will be undertaken by a sub contractor (or the contractor) at an
existing facility. It is however equally likely that they will be carnied out at a new site.
Possible adverse iinpacts may include: soil contamination, increased accident risk from heavy
truck movements and possible modification to local water resource availability. The quantity
of water required is not excessive, but given the severe constraints on available local supplies
the actual location of the yard may interfere with water availability to surrounding properties.

(iv)   Equipment Maintenance and Cleaning
As with the prefabrication yard, it is possible that contractor will utilise locally available
suppliers for equipment and lease equipment as required. In such cases there will be no need
for the creation of a new maintenance yard.
However, should the contractor wish to establish a new facility, the major threat would be the
pollution of groundwater resources from  the industrial waste will be generated by the
maintenance and cleaning activities. Of particular concern is the disposal of wastewater from
the cleaning of equipment since such water will contain pollutants. At a minimum, industrial
strength detergents and oil cleaning solvents.
There will, in addition, be a potential risk of pollution from the accidental spillage of, and, or
leakage from, industrial materials stored on site. The nature and significance of the threat to
water resources cannot be determined at this stage, without full knowledge of the materials to
be stored.
(v)    Borrow Material and Aggregate Washing and Screening
Substantial quantities of borrow material and Aggregates will need to be obtained and made
suitable for use on site. This issue is discussed in greater detail in Section 5.3.2.
At all off site facilities it will be important that contractors maintain and manage sites to high
standards, that health and safety regulations are in force, that appropriate means for waste
control and disposal are applied and that all temporary project sites are cleaned up and made
good. Possible residual wastes of concern at each facility are as follows:

Access to the construction sites, which may require the use of local access roads that would
generally not be considered suitable for use by heavy vehicles will also be a potential source of
concern. Use by construction traffic of such roads may cause damage to the road surface or in
other cases to structures, delays to non-construction traffic and increased risk of accidents.
For off site facilities where some choice in siting is available, direct access to the primary net-
work should be a major criteria in location.
5.2.3     Permanent Impacts
All the project components are noteworthy for having:
* no requirement for additional land acquisition
* no requirement for property take
* no requirement for population relocation or resettlement
* no requirement for impacts on cultural heritage
All works will be carried out within existing public rights of way.
Similarly, with one minor exception (cesspit cleaning operations discussed in Section 5.3.3
below), there will be almost no productive loss, loss of employment or other loss of income
from project actions.
There is therefore no requirement for the definition of compensation rights and policies
beyond those that may be applied for damage done to property and assets during construction
work.
5.2.4     Operational Impacts
There are a few adverse or positive operational impacts common to both projects, and those
that do occur are of sufficient significance to warrant detailed attention in each Project Case
5.3       SANITATION PROJECTS
5.3.1     Pre Construction
There are no pre construction impacts additional to those defined in Section 5.2.1 above.
5.3.2     Construction Impacts (Temporary)
Five areas of activity that may generate potentially significant inpacts are reviewed below.
(i)    Cesspit Removal
In total, project components are estimated to require the closure, excavation, fill and
stabilisation of a potential maximum 7,100 cesspits (2,900 in Akamah, 1,800 in Sawad
Hanash, 1,500 in Safiyah East and 900 in Saba'een). The actual number may be as low as
Y9701                                 -5.6-                             March 1999



Sanaa Water Supply and Sanitation Services          Sana a 1 Environmental Impact Assessment
3,500 (1,500 in Akamah). These actions will bring with them a number of potentially
significant impacts.
* The general threat to public health from the opening of a pit
Some of these pits are deep, 20 m+, (though an average of 15 m is expected) and lie within
public rights of way. Each individual pit, when opened, will therefore be a source of potential
hazard to all uses of that right of way.
It is also common in Sana'a for children to be seen playing around recently dug cesspit holes
and more especially on the mounds of excavated earth. These mounds are both unstable and
unsanitary. The specific nature of any materials to be excavated from around active pits is
unknown but they will undoubtedly pose a much greater health hazard than that excavated for
new sites, with children in particular at risk.
* Nuisance (and potential health effects) from the extreme odour of the open pits.
The nature of the works suggest that nuisance values, especially from odour (and potentially
health concerns), will be high and will be experienced by residents, land users and road users
alike.
* The removal and disposal of waste materials from cesspits.
All (non-solid) materials in existing cesspits will need to be removed and carted away to a
point of safe disposal. Operators of desludgers and other workers in the immediate environs of
the operation will be at some risk from contact with the material and thus at some health risk.
Although some 7,100 pits are expected to be cleared over the project duration the major
concern is over the waste from the 2,900 pits from the Akamah Project. (i.e. prior to
completion and operation of the new WWTP). Nevertheless, the total quantity of material
involved is expected to be quite significant, (say as a worst case 90 m3 per day for a 14-month
period, Table 5.2) and much of this material will be extremely aggressive. It will be anaerobic
and potentially very acidic. It may therefore have a strong adverse impact if disposed of
directly to existing, main or interceptor sewers, or directly to the environment.
Contract documentation requires that for the first four months of the construction period the
materials collected are transferred to the sump of the raw sewage inlet pump at the existing
treatment plant, where it is to be discharged. After this period, the new WWTP is expected to
be operational.
In these circumstances, assuming that the pits contain a volume waste of around half that in
the calculation in Table 5.2, removal would require two trucks for Akamah rising to a fleet of
five for the other contracts.
In the worst-case scenario, it is assumed that 90 mn3 per day of sewage will result in 9,000 ti3
over the four months that will be discharged into the existing WWTP, which equates to a
maximum additional load of 1.5% of design capacity of 6,000 n3. This will be released directly
into the input sump of the WWTP during off-peak hours (i.e. at night), when the plant is not
operating at full capacity.

(ii)   The  collection, handling  and  disposal of waste  generated  by  households
disconnected from their disposal system.
This is another potentially problematic issue even though it is anticipated that no more than
three trenches will be open at any one time and that each trench will be open for a niaximum
of one hundred metres (assumed maximum distance between manholes).
Nevertheless, assuming a plot/structure frontage of 25 m and 4 households per frontage, a
system of temporary sewage collection and disposal will be required to be in place for up to 16
families, say 1 10 persons, throughout the construction period.
The volumes dealt with are quite small4, at a minimum, around 4.5 m3/day and the maximum,
9.7 m3/day but the systems must be in place and it is in the maintenance and management of
operation of those systems that the problems may lie. It will be unacceptable for any raw
domestic sewage to be discharged (or escape) in the public domain.
(iii)  Inappropriate Disposal of Waste Materials
Associated with elements of (i) and (ii) is the problem of fly dumping;. Much of the above
discussion has highlighted the scale of the wastewater, slurry and sludge evacuation
programme required and indirectly the costs (fleet operations) of that operation. Under these
circumstances there is a potential (official and unofficial) for materials to dumped at sites other
than those defined.
Such dumping, (especially of slurry and sludge) will have the potential to cause extensive
damage to virtualy any environment to which it is discharged, particularly if it is permitted for
an extended period. In all cases such dumping will pose a serious health hazard.
Strict controls wiDl therefore be required to ensure that this does not occur.
(iv)   Use of Wastewaters
Farmners are at present using a substantial quantity of waste material available from the WWTP
site, including the sludge and waters. While they use these materials in the knowledge that they
are essentially 'not healthful' neither they nor the authorities are able to determine precisely
the threats posed. Nevertheless their use is accepted practice and it must be assumed that the
risk associated with this is accepted.
However, if the cesspit waste leads to a significant change in that risk, (more heavily
contaminated materials in greater concentration) problems may occur. These may apply both
4 At a design return rate of 36.8 I/cId, (46 ]/cld @ 0.8 return factor) this equates to 4.6 m3/day in total and at

dealing with the direct consumption of the properties affected. In this case they are all assumed to be
residences of 4 households and with 7 persons per household.

to direct users, and potentially secondary and tertiary handlers and users of products
generated.
(v)    Soil Contamination
It is clear that the soils of much of the construction area are contaminated by wastewater. This
contamination will be manifest in two ways:
-     through alteration of the chemical make up of the soils; increased acidity or alkalinity
-     by the presence of pathogens (and nuisance odours) in the excavated material
It is understood that in both cases the contamination is not self-perpetuating. It is reversible
and the reversion process will begin inimediately the source of contamination is removed.
The chemical process will be slower.
Given the above, no long term threat to pipe integrity is expected. Similarly, there is not
expected to be a (abnormal) need to stabilise, or replace soils in the work corridors that have
been affected by exfiltrating waters.
The primary concern will therefore be to the Public Health of anyone exposed to the materials.
Although pathogens once exposed to the air and temperature would be expected to die off
within 72 hours, at most 96 hours, the issue of exposure (especially of the workforce) to
contaminated soils during these periods is of concem.
(vi)   Access and Traffic Disruption
D Property Access
Access to all properties in areas to be served by the project will be adversely affected at some
point in the construction period. In most cases these impacts will be minor, restricted both in
their severity and duration by the narrowness of the trenches required for the relatively small
diameter of the pipes being laid.
However, in cases where construction work is slated to take place in the immediate vicinity of
special sites, (schools mosques and such like,) or other Public Buildings specific access issues
will be raised.
* Traffic Disruption
For larger pipes (>500mm) these impacts will be far greater, magnified by the potential depth
of the construction works and their duration. These pipes by their nature, also tend to be laid
in main roads or secondary roads of functional importance to local and urban wide road
networks.
In these cases, potentially severe impacts on traffic circulation (delays etc.), local access and
plot access, can be expected with impacts resulting not only from a loss of road space as a
direct result of the construction site but also to construction traffic, loading and unloading,
parking etc. The areas and roads most likely to be severely affected are shown on Figure 5.1.

for additional closure and diversion over relatively small distances, say 200m or less. On
primary roads, the probable scope of ongoing works will be such that it is unlikely that vehicle
waiting areas will add to required traffic control measures.
(vii) Wadi Sailah
Wadi Sailah runs north - south through Sana'a, passing in the vicinity of the existing WWTP,
Figure 5.2. It is proposed to use the wadi as an alignment for two mains utilised in the Safiyah
East and Saba'een Projects (part of the Long-term Program).
The 1978 Master Plan proposed to lay out a park along the entire (planned) urban stretch of
the Wadii but this has not happened. Further studies in 1995 and 1996 stressed the significance
of Wadi Sailah to the urban ecology of Sana'a and recommended a further project to protect it
from a variety of developmental problems. These concerns were highlighted in Section 4.2.4
and summarised in Table 4.2. The pipelines of Sections 8 and 9 (Figure 5.2) are currently
under construction under the MOPD project

5.3.3     Operations Phase
In this sub-section both negative and positive Project impacts are defined. Potential negative
impacts from the Operational Phase are reviewed under four broad headings:
* Use of Existing Lines
* Use of the Existing Treatment Plant
* Hydrogeology
* Social Impacts
Project Benefits are discussed as Direct, or Non Attributable benefits.
(i)    Use of Existing Sewer Lines
Project designs call for the connection of the new networks to elements of the existing sewer
system. Such cases are located on Figure 5.3. Project specifications do not call for the
replacement of these lengths of sewer line or for any form of works other than connection.
This is understood to result from discussions with NWSA, who consider the existing lines to
be fundamentally sound. However, there is no physical evidence available that these pipes are
sound and in the absence of inspection data and or regular maintenance records, is possible
that either:
-     Pipes in the ground are not sized as reported and are too small to accept the design
flow, or
- -   Pipes are damaged to the extent that may suffer excess leakage, or more likely,
blockage, with consequent backfiow effects and flooding.
The former case is considered most unlikely. All pipes to which the new systems are to be
connected were located from 'As Built' drawings certified by a reputable International
Engineering Consultancy.
The latter case is more likely and may occur with resulting severe, but generally localised,
flooding impacts. This is more an issue with regard to the main interceptors. The Sector
Development Plan (SDP) contains a recommendation for a project to rehabilitate thirty-five
km of interceptors, (which are only 10 to 12 years old), subject to confirmation through
CCTV inspection.
(ii)   Use of Treatment Plant
The phasing of Sector development works is such that wastewater will be collected and
transported to the existing WWTP during the first four months of the construction period.
Following this period the new WWTP will be operational.
This plant comprises six stabilisation ponds and was initially designed for an inflow of
6,000 m3 per day. -It now receives some 15,600 m3 per day. Appendix C presents a situation
analysis of the plant and a mitigation plan.
Two standby generators to supply power to the inlet pumps during periods of blackout are out
of order and are in urgent need of repair or replacement.

initial four months of the construction phase. It is therefore expected that the impact of the
additional load will be minimal.
There is no site within the defined project areas that is known to be producing effluent that is
hazardous or which would be a source of specific concem. Accordingly adverse impacts will
be restricted to those that would typically be attributed to domestic wastewater.
Once completed the new WWTP will have ample capacity to accommodate all projected flows
(Table 5.4) and will treat waters to desired standards.

The new WWTP is designed for an average flow of 50,000 m3/day (580 lls). The peak design
flow is 1,740 I/s. The plant is designed, based on analysis of raw sewage, for the following
influent characteristics:
BOD - 500 mg/

NH4      100 mgl
6 This discussion applies only to the Akamah Project.
Y9701                                 -5.17-                           March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
The treatment process (activated sludge) will result in the effluent having BOD better than
30 mg/ and SS better than 30 mg/I, which is within the WHO guidelines for general crop
irrigation7.
The new WWTP will produce 80 tonnes per day of dried sludge at a moisture content of 30%,
when operating at full capacity. Initially, the plant will be operating at around 40% capacity,
producing approximately 32 tonnes per day. The sludge will largely be stabilised by aerobic
digestion within the activated sludge plant and will be given further treatment by thickening
and drying on beds before storage/composting for six months in a sludge storage area. The
treatment and storage processes will render the sludge essentially free of pathogens. A review
of impact, extracted from the new WWTP Design Report and Review of Impact, is presented
in Appendix D. Dried sludge will be sold to farmers, as is the practice in the current pond
WWTP. A formal wastewater and sludge reuse study will be performed and financed as part
of the project.
(iii) Hydrogeology
Large scale leakage during project operations may promote localised raising of water tables
which could adversely affect the function of existing percolation pits in unsewered areas on the
periphery of the new systems, promoting overflow, mixing of waters and contaminating
shallow aquifer. Rising water tables may also pose a long-term threat to building foundations.
However such concerns are unlikely to be of significance. They will be highly localised and not
widespread as at present and may be readily dealt with on a case by case basis as they occur. If
systems are not maintained, some increase in intensity of impact (even over present levels)
may be experienced at localised sites of system failure.
(iv)   Social Impacts
* Migration and Social Organisation
The preparation, construction and operation of the projects is unlikely to generate adverse
impacts from in-migration to Sana'a or, specifically, to the project areas as:
-     there will be little or no requirement to import labour for the project.
-     though improved sanitary conditions may increase the attractiveness of the
areas for development they will not encourage large-scale relocation of populations
either within or to the Sana'a urban area.
* Income and Employment
With no property or productive land take the only losses in income and employment will be to
those limited number of private contractors that have been providing cesspit clearance services
to local residents. The losses incurred are estimated in the ESS (for Sawad Hanash and
Akamah) at about $90,000 per annum or $2.00 per capita.
7 Reuse of Effluents: Methods of Wastewater Treatment and Safeguards, WHO Technical Paper Series, No.
517, 1973.

These costs will be incurred by very few individuals. Moreover, while the projects will clearly
have a short-term impact on business they will not entirely eliminate demand.
(v)    Development Constraints
The project is unlikely to significantly alter existing patterns of development directly though it
is conceivable that modifications to land values (see Housing and Urban Development in (vi)
below) and potential future pressures on the available quantity of serviced land will stimulate a
change in the land use pattern of the improved areas.8
However, the design specifications utilised are based on existing general patterns of use and
are the product of a need to balance service provision against costs. Thus while they will
accommodate some very limited intensification of land use and hence rates of water use they
will not cope with radical changes.

(vi)   Direct Project Benefits
The design horizon for the Project is year 2020. The total projected population served by the
Sanitation project is around 106,500. These populations will gain a number of directly related
benefits as follows:
* Public Health Improvements
The ESS household Survey (of Akamah and Sawad Hanash) results indicate that over 60% of
respondents believed that tap water was contaminated by sewerage and that 12% actively took
measures at cost (filtering) to counter the perceived contamination. In addition more than one
third of households boiled water on a daily basis (excluding boiling in the preparation of food
and beverages).
It is not possible to state definitively at this time whether the perception of contamination is
correct9 or, whether or not any contaminant source will be removed by the Project. However
it may be expected that after project construction, households previously concerned about
contamination will perceive the threat diminished, if not removed, and reduce or eliminate
their preventative expenditure. Clearly this is only a benefit if the water is in fact not
contaminated and households do not later regret their decision not undertake precautionary
measures.
- Income and Employment
Secondary employment opportunities generated by a growth in the local informal and formal
sectors of the economy, most notably in the commercial sectors that will stem from the
improved physical environment of affected areas and the reduction in the proportion of
household expenditure on sanitation.
At present, degraded sanitary conditions are claimed by local business owners to seriously
impact turnover. In areas prone to sewage flooding turnover is reported to decline by 60-90%
during flood events.
* Housing and Urban Development
-     Land and Property Values
Though land and property markets in Yemen are ill developed and may not be that responsive
to changes in urban environmental quality, some increase in land and property values may be
expected from Project generated relief.
Realtors interviewed in the preparation of the ESS suggest that property values may rise by
30-50% as a direct consequence of the project and undeveloped land by as much as 50-100%.
This view is to some extent supported by the householders themselves. Some 400/%o
9 Water put in the system by NWSA is clean but whether or not it is contaminated when received by
consumers is unknown. Tests on tap water collected at the timc. aE the ESS showed no significant
contamination.
'0 The remainder were split 21% disagreed and 37% did not know.

considered connection to a sewage network would substantially increase property values, and
the average benefit defined by those able to give a value (63% of the 40%), averaged just over
50%.
-     Reduced Protective Expenditure
It is argued that further benefits are likely to be obtained from the reduction in household
repairs and or protective works (against sewage inflow). Approximately 25% of households in
Akamah and Sawad Hanash claimed to have spent money either protecting their property
against incursion or in repairs to damage done. The estimated total value of the works was put
at just over $200,000.
* Wastewater Tariffs
Against these benefits should be set the increase in costs for Sanitation Services. At present
NWSA charge a flat rate fee of YR 5,000 ($38.46) for household connections to the sewage
network. Tariffs to be introduced in December 1998 are as in Table 5.6.

(vii) Non Attributable Benefits
There are also likely to be a number of benefits derived beyond those directly attributable to
local populations: These will include:
* Reduced pavement damage and thus reduced VOCs and road maintenance costs;
* Tourism potential: The tourism sector in Yemen has grown rapidly in recent years and is
now worth some $50million per annum mostly in hard currencies. These would be virtually
eliminated by a major health scare, or epidemic.

5.4.1     Pre Construction
There are no pre construction imnpacts defined additional to those in Section 5.2.1 above.

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assesiment
5.4.2     Construction Phase
In general, the impacts of construction activities for the water supply project will be less
severe than those for the Sewage Projects. Trenches are shallower and narrower, and pipes
smaller. Impacts on road users will also be less severe with the greater majority of works
confined to local neighbourhoods. Therefore, in addition to having less overall effect, adverse
impacts will be more keenly felt by those directly benefiting from the project and will be made
the more tolerable for being so.
Perhaps most significantly there will be no requirement for cesspit clearance. A programme to
sewer the entire project area has recently been completed and all cesspits dug out and
stabilised. There is nonetheless one area of potential concern: system flushing and disinfection.
Flushing and Disinfection
The contract documents require that the contractor flush and disinfect pipe systems. They
further specify:
-     Use of one of: Calcium or Sodium Hypochlorite, or Chlorine Gas
-     Dosage rates
-     Residual concentrations of after first flush
-     Maximum residual after second flush
These requirements introduce a number of possible areas of concern.
(i)    Storage and Use of Materials
All three materials are potentially hazardous. They can
-     cause very severe external damage to all who come into contact with them
-     cause death if ingested (or inhaled )
-     cause substantial damage to all component elements of physical environments, plant
life, fauna, and especially micro-organisms
The potential threat posed by the use of these materials is summarised in Table 5.7 for each of
the stages of the use process.
A further factor that would apply to all aspects of Table 5.7 (except fly dumping) is the speed
and the adequacy of the response to an event. Prompt effective actio-n would in most cases
effectively eliminate adverse impacts and in the few remaining instances would minimise the
severity of the impacts.
(ii)   Disposal of Wastewaters
The waters utilised in this process will contain significant quantities of residual chlorine, in
concentrations of not less than Sppm after the first flushing, and not more than Ippm on the
final flush. While these waters will not pose a significant threat to human health, they will not
be suitable for consumption, and as such will need to be disposed of in a manner that precludes
local populations accessing even residual quantities.
Y9701                                   -5.22-                             March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services          Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
The wastes will however be toxic to a wide variety of organisms and highly destructive to
many micro-environments, a factor that should be considered in their disposal.
(iii) Resource Use
In addition to the above flushing and disinfection (and pressure testing) would impose very
short and highly localised, but nonetheless potentially significant, water resource demands on
local networks.
5.4.3     Operations Phase
(i)    Existing System
The existing networks, both official and unofficial, in place across much of the project area
will potentially generate a number of concerns.
* Firstly, wherever possible normal" supplies to existing users will have to be maintained,
throughout the construction period.
Water supply provision from the public system in Sana'a is already poor and the cost of
alternative supply sources high (at least for guaranteed clean water) Accordingly, extended
periods of supply loss may impose substantial costs on affected households, financially,
physically and socially.
There are however no compelling reasons for such impacts to occur. Contract documents
require existing supply provision to be maintained and there is nothing in the nature of the
existing system or the development proposals that should make such it difficult for any
contractor to meet this obligation.
* Secondly, although there is a requirement for existing systems and new systems to be
isolated and although existing cesspits have been removed there is a slight risk of (cross)
contamination. Accordingly, care must be taken that elements of the existing system are not
left around after completion of the works or that some households or blocks of houses are
left unconnected.
* Thirdly, all existing consumers must be guaranteed connection to the new network. No
reduction in existing access to piped water, (whether legal or illegal) would be an
acceptable impact.
* Finally, while it is expected that the ad hoc systems will be isolated from supply, every
effort should be made to ensure that these do not simply reappear. It may be, for example,
that while many people welcome the project and the improved service it will bring, there
will be a number who do not want to pay for water.
" Normal in this context implies that the areas in question should recive their theoretical typical supply
pattern of the last few years, during construction. They should not be further disadvantaged.

(ii)   Wastewater Generation
Though there can be no doubt of the benefit from increased available water supplies to a
population they also bring about an associated increase in the quantity of wastewater
generated'2.
Though the entire project area has recently been sewered (works completed in May 1997), this
has particularly important consequences in that it will place significant additional load on
already inadequate waste disposal systems, further increasing the potential for downstream
environrental damage prior to the completion of the new WWTP (see Section 5.3.3 (ii)
above).
(iii) Cross Contamination
The recent sewering of the area will ensure that the expected rise in the level of wastewater
generation will not cause problems of cesspit overflow as is common throughout many areas
of the city at present. Accordingly, the risk of cross cont,amination will be markedly reduced
from present levels even under conditions of relatively poor maintenance assuming that the
design requirements for separation are maintained in construction.

6.        MMTIGATION PLAN
6.1       INTRODUCTION
The remaining sections of this report, Sections 6 and 7, contain the Environmental
Management Plan (EMP) for the Project.
This Section outlines the Mitigation Plan. In general terms, it comprises the three elements
outlined in Sections 6.2 to 6.4:
-     Consultation Programme (Section 6.2)
-     Measures to be adopted for the Sanitation Projects (Section 6.3). These include the
general guidelines for construction activities (on site and off site) and the specific
measures proposed in response to the impacts of concern outlined in Section 5. Given
the substantially different status of the various sanitation projects, the emphasis in this
section is on the mitigation of impacts directly associated with the Akamah Project that
may be applied in all subsequent project components'.
-     Specific mitigation requirements related to the Hasabah Water Supply Project, that are
additional to those outlined for the Sanitation Projects, Section 6.4.
Section 6.5 contains a tabular summary of the Mitigation Plan in the context of the impacts
defined in the previous section.
6.2    CONSULTATION PROGRAMME
6.2.1     Proarammes
Despite the fact that this type of category "B" environmental project does not require a public
consultation program, the Government of Yemen has nevertheless implemented an extensive
program of public awareness and consultation. This program comprises three components: (i)
public meetings; (ii) newspaper and television articles and programs; and (iii) household
surveys. Each of these is summarised below, and details are included in Tables 6.1 and 6.2.
(i)    Public Meetings
To date there have been 4 public meetings about the project. The first was on July 26 1997,
and the latest took place on February 20 1999. Another meeting is scheduled before the start
of construction, and further meetings are planned during the construction period. On average,
these public meetings are attended by about 30 to 35 people living in the affected
neighbourhoods. Topics discussed typically include the deterioration of sewerage conditions,
the duration of construction, and the number of households served.
At this time, there are no characteriscs or conditions associated vwth other Sanitation project components
that would suggest additional, or special measures beyond those defined for Akamah, need be developecL

(iii) Rights to receive the services provided
It is important that all members of the community in the Project areas are entitled to the same
minimum standard of service. It will not be acceptable for some members of the community to
receive no, or less than the minimum, service.
It will be necessary therefore for specific details of project entitlements to be made available to
all members of the public. In addition it will be necessary to inform the public what
mechanisms are available to them for ensuring they receive the minimum service. As above,

-     to which office they may make further complaint if the problem remains unresolved.
In each of the above cases the information required will be provided in written form and
verbally at Project community meetings.
Pamphlets will be provided to each affected household prior to commencement of civic works
and will be available at the Project Office. The pamphlets will be prepared by the PMU and
supervising engineer, and will give general information to affected communities. Television
and radio advertisements will also be utilised, as well as newspaper articles.
Community leaders, NGOs and others will be invited to attend Project Meetings as required
during which they will be provided with the literature and explanations of its content. These
groups will be expected to disseminate this information through all the channels open to them.

6.3.2     Construction On Site
(i)    General
Appendix E to this report provides broad guidelines for good construction practice that may
be included if required in potential contract negotiations. Adherence to these guidelines would
largely eliminate the majority of construction related impacts.
The following text therefore addresses only those issues that are of particular concern for the
projects assessed, or which are not usually encountered in the construction process.
(ii)   Public Safety
The major public health (and safety) concerns are addressed below. However there are a
number of measures that could be undertaken on site to limit the risk of an accident involving
the public.
-     trenches should be open for as little time as possible.
-     appropriate trench crossing facilities should be provided in sufficient number to permit
safe crossing of trenches to gain access to private properties.
appropriate alternative pedestrian routes, that separate pedestrians from road traffic,
should be provided. Where restrictions in roadway space are such that the risk of
pedestrian - vehicular conflict is significantly heightened, temporary closure of the
access may be considered.
-     use of temporary fences to isolate the deeper (>3m) trenches.
-     the use of all heavy and mobile equipment shall be strictly controlled with all drivers
and operators required to be licensed and all heavy equipment operated in strict
accordance with manufacturers guidelines or Yemeni Guidelines. Particular note shall
be made by site inspectors of incidences where equipment requiring two man crews or
driver and observers are operated by one man.
(iii) Cesspit Removal
The basic construction guidelines will need to be expanded to address the public safety
concerns at cesspit removal sites, as follows:
During operations each site shall include specific measures to prevent casual public access to
the site. This should if necessary include the provision of temporary fences or barriers. Prior to
construction community leaders should be advised of the details of the work in their area, its
extent, duration and the hazards it poses. The threat to children should be emphasised.
During periods of non operation:
-     Wherever possible pits shall not be left open at night. They should either be backfilled,
or if that is not possible an open pit shall be covered with materials that can not be
casually removed.
Y9701                                   -6.5-                              March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
-      lPedestrian safety problems are to be minimised by excluding the public from areas of
construction activity and ensuring that adequate and safe alternative access is provided.
-     Watchmen or security personnel shall patrol each construction site containing one or
rnore open pits.
-     Specific and appropriate warning notices will be required to advise motorists of the
hazard. Physical barriers that will prevent unauthorised vehicular access to the site
should supplement this.
-     )Pumping of cesspit waste into WWTP should be undertaken during low-flow periods.
(iv)   Public Health
As noted above, prior to the initiation of the construction of the project the population shall be
advised of the nature and extent of the works proposed.
As part of that programme the Public shall be made aware of the potential additional threats to
their health that may be generated and measures available to reduce risks. They will be advised
of what to do in the event of a person falling victim to one or other threat. Options will
include:
self-treatment options, where available
where to obtain medical attention, and when.
A number of hospitals and clinics currently exist in and around the project area as seen in
Figure 6.1. The hospitals-have emergency rooms and are attended by doctors and medical
staff 24 hours a day. The clinics are open between 8 and 12 hours a day and are staffed by
qualified medical staff.
If possible, as part of the project it would be appropriate to establish a clinic (within an
existing facility) dealing with populations from affected areas. This clinic could also be cited as
the point of contact for project affected persons for obtaining health advisory services. It need
not be necessary for the clinic to be open on a permanent basis or to have a qualified doctor
available at all open times. A health professional should however be available at all times.
(v)   Employee Health and Safety
-     Soil Contamination
Prior to construction a number of test samples should be carried out to detennine the nature
and severity of the contamination of soils in which the contractors staff will work2. The soils
should be tested for:
-     Microbiological contamination (mainly pathogens)
-     Soil acidity and chemical contamination.
2 The information gathered will also help in the prepaation of the informaton materials.
Y9701                                  -6.6-                              March 1999



Sana a Water Supply and Sanitation Services          Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
Once analysed the threat to workers should be classified into one of three categories:
-     Nil
-     Minimal Risk
-     Threat to both the workforce and the pipeline.
In the case of minimal risk simple mitigation measures such as the wearing of appropriate
clothing and education of the workforce of the risk and preventative measures proposed
would be sufficient.
If a significant threat to health is identified tests should be retaken to confirm the threat and to
establish rates of die off of pathogens. It would be expected that simple exposure will be
sufficient treatment. However this will increase the number of pits open at any one time with
significant adverse impacts elsewhere.
In the case of a defined persistent threat the area in the immnediate vicinity of the pipe should
be excavated and the waste material carted off to a dump site approved by NWSA and EPA.
Approved methods of handling and disposal will be required.
(vi)   Collection, Storage and Disposal of Household Wastewater
During construction it is anticipated that up to 9 m3 per day of wastewater will be generated
by households temporarily disconnected from their cesspits (Section 5.3.2). This waste must
be collected, stored, transported to the existing WWTP and disposed of at the inlet sump of
raw sewage inlet pump. These are clear requirements of the bid documents.
Of concern here is to ensure that operations to:
-     connect the temporary system,
-     store wastewaters,
-     empty to tanker,
-     disconnect the temporary system,
-     move system, and connect to permanent system,
are carried out in a manner that prevents (or mininiises) spillage of wastewater and in the
event of spill that there are adequate means available to contain it and neutralise it.
This will require the establishment of a unit (or as many as required by the contractor to meet
his work schedule) within the construction work force specifically trained for carrying out
these works. No other workers shall be permitted to handle or use the equipment.

(vii) Removal and Disposal of Liquid Wastes
The materials from the 2,900 pits of the Akamah Project may be extremely aggressive. It will
be anaerobic and probably very acidic and will have a strong adverse impact if disposed of
directly to existing main or interceptor sewers, or directly to the environment.
For the first four months of the construction phase, this material must be taken to the existing
WWTP and placed directly into the inlet sump (Figure 6.2). The removal and disposal of the
liquid waste must be carried out during low (off peak) flow periods into the existing WWTP
to reduce the possibility of surcharging it.
Given that the emptying of cesspit waste will only increase the load on the WWTP by 1.5% of
operating capacity (Section 5.3.3), and given that any loading will be done during low-flow
periods, it is unlikely that the disposal of cesspit waste into the WWTP will have any
significant adverse impacts. However, to ensure that the operational efficiency of the WWTP
is not further (or permanently) impaired it may be necessary for some form of dilution of the
sludge and slurry prior to disposal. The efficiency of the WWTP will be monitored by the on-
site office and the ESO as part of the monitoring programme. The ESO will determine
whether or not dilution becomes necessary.
The liquid waste will be placed in the inlet sump to be lifted by electrically driven pumps. The
standby generators must, therefore, be repaired and/or replaced to remove the risk of overflow
during periods of power outages. A sum of $55,000 has been allocated to cover the
repair/replacement of one generator.
To ensure that fly tipping is minimised, site supervisors must be responsible for registering all
vehicles that leave the cohstruction site full. A counterpart supervisor at the WWTP will
register the vehicle disposing of its load. The WWT? supervisor will also be responsible for
ensuring that all wastes are disposed of corTectly.
To ensure that existing users of wastewaters and other wastes at the WWTP are not adversely
affected, there will be a requirement for NWSA to undertake an education/ information
programme advising users of the possible changes in material and its possible impacts on
public health / crops / water quality etc.
Further, it will be necessary to monitor the output of the WWTP to ensure that there is at least
some understanding of the nature of the materials being used, (See Section 7.3.1).

(viii) Fill Material Storage and Use
The quantities of fill material required to be on site at any one time will require that effective
site management be in place. For indicative purposes it is assumed that some 45 m3 per day
will be required for use on site. Given the urban nature of the site and the relative speed with
which the construction site will proceed upstream, it is recommended that contractors be
required to bring and use material, without temporary storage, on site.
Where necessary temporary storage may be permitted provided that:
-     it is for a maximum period of 5 days.
-     all existing access is maintained.
-     no residual material is left at site.
-     heaps are located at all times in areas to be temporarily acquired for construction
purposes.
-     heaps are not located within immediate proximity to any pit or operational trench. In
such cases, "immediate proximity" would be a distance that allowed for immediate
movement of material from the heap to the pit or trench, or where children playing on
the heap would be in danger of tumbling into the excavation.
(ix)   Access Management
For Akamah, a total of some 105 truck movements a day could be required to meet the
combined cesspit emptying and fill import requirements identified in (vii) and (viii) above (a
total of 155 movements at the other sites). Assuming that at any one time 5O0/o of the required
fleet is travelling and 25% is at either origin point approximately 25 trucks will be parked or
accessing the construction site.
In all cases special management measures will need to be in place to ensure access and egress
to the site is managed without damaging surrounding properties and, where required, to
maintain public access.
In most cases, in local neighbourhoods, it would be recommended that individual roads are
closed for vehicular traffic and pedestrian movement, and property access is catered for by
defined routes clearly marked on site. Where roads are not closed measures as outlined in (ii)
above will be required to protect public safety and as in section (x) below for traffic
management.
Very specific measures will be required to ensure that all access points to a site are supervised
and that drivers and operators respect the specific regulations on speed, routes etc. defined in
an approved access plan.
The approved access plan should be defined in a plan and on the ground. The document
should outline:
-     permitted access routes, by vehicle type
-     permitted times of use of routes
-     speeds on access routes
-     site control, parking and unloading areas

(xi)   Abatement of Noise and Air Pollution

For the stockpile yard, assuming no storage of hazardous materials, the only requirements are

-     direct frontage access to the primary road
-     via a secondary public road assessed as suitable for the heavy truck movements

width of the road, its surface and condition, lines of sight and the junction with the
primary network.
Sites located beyond these parameters should be the subject of individual review.
The prefabrication yard will require access to adequate water supplies and immnediate access
to suitable (for heavy traffic) roads. Location criteria for the yard will be as for the stockpile
yard with the addition that it should as far as is possible be located to:
-     minimise additional dust and noise nuisance values (beyond ambient levels) on
surrounding populations,
-     limit the potential impact on surrounding populations of the water storage and use
requirements. In this context, NWSA should be required to review and approve the
water sources to be used and the proposed size of any storage facilities.
The maintenance equipment yard will be the source of the most concem. It will potentially
generate a number of wastes (solid and liquid) that should be collected, stored and disposed of
in an approved manner.
To facilitate this prior to operations the contractor shall be required to list the materials to be
used at site and define the procedures to be applied for waste control and management. If
equipment is to be leased, lessors should be informed that their facilities will be subject to
inspection and audit (see Section 7.3.1).
Where possible the contractor should be encouraged to recover, treat and re use materials, in
particular lubricants.
Accidental spill and leakage of chemicals stored on site could pose a serious potential threat to
water resources and public health. Accordingy it is proposed that the contractor be required
to submit to the NWSA an inventory of the materials to be stored on site, together with a
preliminary indication of the quantities of material that may be present at any one time.
Clearly these indications of quantity will by necessity, be indicative only. NWSA should, if
necessary, consult with specialist units in government for advice on the precautionary
measures to be taken in terms of both the planning and constuction of the camp and the
required procedures for the storage and handling of such material.
They should then inform the Contractor of these requirements. It is expected that these will
include, at a minimum, specific operational requirements such as:

(ii)   Wastewater Treatment Plant
Items (vi) and (vii) in Section 6.3.2 require the use of the existing Wastewater Treatment Plant
(WWTP) for the disposal of collected waste materials.
Specific guidelines are laid down for the disposal of this waste. It will be necessary however
for a temporary site office to be established at the plant to allow for the supervision of the
disposal. This may be staffed by staff from the engineering supervisors or by NWSA
personnel. The supervisor will be responsible for ensuring that:
-      all waste is dumped at the location required and during the periods required.
-     registering the quantity of waste discharged (license plate and volume) to allow for
monitoring of wastes.
-      equipment at the site, such as generators and pumps, are maintained and repaired as
necessary.
-     aeration devises may be used, if feasible, to improve treatment effectiveness.
Records of the waste leaving a construction site (and those discharging at the WWTP) shall be
monitored by the supervising engineer to ensure that they match.

6.3.4     Construction: Resource Use
Two forms of resource use are anticipated:
(i)    Water Use; On and Off site
Significant quantities of water will be utilised on site in construction. In this context, local
communities may be affected in two ways:
-     the quantity of water available from  their water sources has been reduced by
construction activities.
-     their water sources are contaminated.
Contractors should be required to bring all water on site in tankers and be allowed to fill their
tankers only at sites defined in agreement with NWSA. These may be new facilities located at
construction yards, in communities, or pre existing facilities.
This recommendation is recognised as potentially restrictive. However, such are the costs of
non-NWSA water sources and such is the potential for community concern (and resistance)
that it is considered prudent to eliminate any potential for concern.
Y9701                                    - 6.18-                             March 1999

Off site, NWSA should again be required to approve the water source proposed for use by the
Contractor in the context of existing, local patterns of use, availability of altemative supplies
(for communities or existing users) and the likely peak quantity of water required as measured
against existing consumption pattems.
In general terms the development of new sources (wells) should not be approved unless no
reasonable alternative is available or unless the well is capped and taken out of production by
project end.
(ii)    Borrow material
Borrow material will be required to be transported to site in substantial quantities. Assuming

Akamah alone. Including all projects some 230,000 m3 will need to be imported.

6.3.5     Operations
Four issues of concern are addressed below.
(i)    Use of Existing Sewer Lines
The project calls for existing sewer lines and mains to be utilised to transport the collected
sewage to the WWTP. While these lines are known to be of an appropriate size their condition
is not known.
As a precursor to project implementation it is proposed that all existing main and interceptor
sewer lines to be used by the projects are subject to CCTV investigation. Clearly, given the
concept nature of many of the designs, the extent of the works to be carried out is unknown.
The recomnendations therefore take two forms:
(a)   For Akamah: Preparation of contract documentation for CCTV analysis to be
undertaken for all existing mains and interceptors to be utilised by the Project. CCTV
works to include as a minimum:
1,500 metres of interceptor
2,000 metres of main
The extent of any works required will be deternined following CCTV investigations; therefore
a Provisional sum should be allocated for any works defined as essential.
(b)-  Other Projects: Preparation of contract documentation for CCTV analysis to be
undertaken for existing mains and interceptors as follows:
Intercetor r(n. m)            Mains lin. m)
Sawad Hanash                           Nld                          Ni
