However, as a result of the findings of the Akamah programme, it may be that either:
-     there is a need for a much-reduced scope of CCTV works. The findings suggest that
only the interceptors are of concern, for example, or
-     alternatively, the damage is sufficiently widespread to suggest that pipe replacement is
a preferred option.
Y9701                                  -6.20-                             March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services        Sana 'a I Environmental ImpactAssessment
(ii)  Use of Existing Treatment Plant for Disposal of Domestic Wastes
The adverse impacts of disposal to the existing WWTP are potentially significant. However,
the only complete mitigation available is to delay construction until the new WWTP is
operational. Partial mitigation (reductions in additional cesspit loading) may be achieved by
delaying construction start until there is a reasonable certainty that the WWTP will be
completed in advance of the Akamah works5.
Alternatively it may argued that public health concerns are sufficiently great in the Akamah
area that delays in project implementation could lead to significant additional health costs,
(potentially including unnecessary deaths).
In effect the argument comes down to a single issue. Is more damage caused by disposal of the
wastewaters into the existing WWTP than by a further 4 months unsanitary conditions in the
Akamah Urban Area?
It may be possible to generate a quantitative answer to this question but any such value is
unlikely to have any more validity under intensive scrutiny of the assumptions made than
qualitative measures. It is the subjective view of this report that any incremental damage done
to the environment at and downstream of the WWTP will be relatively slight in comparison to
the quantum of that already done and most importantly is unlikely to be irreversible. Though it
will take time, once the new plant is operational the environment could in theory recover.
The issue is therefore whether the public health threat is greater in Akamah in the without
project case or downstrearn of the existing WWTP in the with project case. It is the opinion of
this report that, due in large part to the minimal impact on the existing WWTP, the public
health threat is greater in Akamah, and that the installation of sewers in Akamah should
proceed as quickly as possible.
(iii)   Re-Use Of Wastewaters and Sludge
The issue here is not the health threat posed by the re-use of wastewaters but the issue of the
strategy on re-use of the collected waters. A significant proportion of the benefits claimed for
sewage projects is that with collection and treatment they facilitate re-use. The benefits
claimed increase proportionately with water scarcity and cost.
Given problems of water scarcity in Sana'a it is therefore considered not unreasonable for any
major investment project in sewage collection to be linked to a plan that details precisely how
the collected (and treated) wastewater is to be utilised. Given this, a formal wastewater and
sludge re-use policy will be developed with assistance from IDA as part of the Sana'a I
Project. This will comprise as a minimum,
* An assessment of the options available for the use of treated waters and dried sludge. These
should include as a minimum,
-     direct re-use within the frainework of a specific of an agricultural project
-     direct re-use of dried sludge as fertiliser by farmers
-     ad hoc direct use by individual uses.
5 And also the Hasabah Water Supply Project Works if possible.
Y9701                                  -6.21-                            March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services           Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
* The creation of an effective framework for the safe use of treated materials.
Specifically there is a requirement for:
-     guidelines for the handling and transport of wastewaters not carried to the WWTP by
sewer lines.
-      guidelines for the use of the these materials for non domestic purposes6
-     guidelines for the storage and disposal of such materials
-     guidelines for the production, handling, storage and sale of produce cultivated with re-
use waters and dried sludge
-     the establishment of a  monitoring  capability sufficiently effective to assure the
population that the waters are safe for re-use and that no potable or domestic waters
have been directly contaminated by re-use products
-     the definition of areas in which treated materials may be utilised subject to the
guidelines developed.
It is understood that the MoAWR, FAO and a Dutch technical assistance team have been
evaluating the opportunities and guidelines for wastewater re-use and have submitted their
findings to the EPC for approval. Discussions with the team that undertook this work indicate
that earlier discussions centred on much less stringent guidelines and that the shift towards
greater control was in large part prompted by the situation in Taiz, where it is reported that
recent extreme water shortages have led to increased use of wastewaters and a sharp rise in
the incidence of gastro-enteritic problems.
6.4    HASABAR WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
6.4.1      Pre-Construction
There is no requirement for mitigation works during the Pre-construction Phase.
6.4.2      Construction
There is only one specific area of construction activity for which additional mitigation
measures7 may be required: Flushing and Disinfection.
Flushing and Disinfection
The contract documents call for the use of Chlorine gas or a solution of Sodium (or Calcium)
hypochlorite in the flushing and cleansing of the newly constructed pipelines.
All three chemicals! are potentially very hazardous and will need to be stored, transported,
handled and disposed of with care.
6 This strategy recognises that cultural factors dictate there will be no circumstances within the time fame
under study whereby treated waters may be utilised for domestic purposes.
7 Additional to those defined for the Sanitation Project Components and in the Construction Guidelines
' It is not believed any contractor will opt for the most hazardous of the three chemicals, Chlorine. This is
expensive and is not readily available in Yemen.
Y9701                                     -6.22.                              Marc J999



Sana a Water Supply and Sanitation Services          Sana 'a I Environmental Impact .4ssessment
It is recommended therefore that a specific unit be established with responsibility for flushing
and cleansing operations:
In addition the following basic approach should be adopted.
-     Materials storage environment to be in strict accordance with manufacturers
specifications.
-     Specific hazardous materials handling and storage procedures to be established (or
reviewed) and an appropriate training programme for staff developed.
-     Access to materials strictly controlled and register kept of use and access.
-     To avoid the risk of casual public contact with the raw chemicals (either through
spillage, vandalism or negligence), materials should be mixed off site, in a controlled
environment, and transported to site in appropriate vehicles.
-     Two man tanker crew trained in the event of an accident
-     HAZOPS procedures should be designed (or reviewed) and staff trained in their use.
Regular testing of contingency planning and procedures is required.
-     All other personnel off site during flushing and disinfection operations.
Under all possible concentrations (initial concentration 5 ppm minimum residual chlorine after
first flushing, maximum residual chlorine 1 ppm after final flush) the disinfection solution
wastewater would be of sufficient concentration to cause long term damage to the WWTP
(kill off of bacteria) if discharged directly to it. Distances to the plant however, are
considerable (7 kin) and it would expected that substantial dilution will occur during the
transmission period.
It is therefore considered reasonable for such materials to be discharged directly to sewers. To
minimise potential damage to the sewers the rate of discharge should not exceed the maximum
design velocity of the sewerage system, 3m/s.
An audit of the chemical storage facility and the equipment to be utilised in injection should be
undertaken prior to first use and at random thereafter and the recommnendations of any audit
implemented prior to any further operational use of the facility if so specified.
As indicated earlier in Section 6.3.4 to minimise potential (albeit negligible) adverse impacts
on the availability of water to local communities, contractors should be required to bring all
water on site in tankers and be allowed to fill their tankers only at sites defined in agreement
with NWSA.

Existing System
To ensure that supplies are maintained at a minimum at present levels the contractor should be
required to ensure that no pipe is removed or disconnected until an alternative temporary
replacement is in place.

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Se"vces         Sanw 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
To ensure that all existing consumers receive supplies from the new network, NWSA should
be required to undertake a programme of surveys and consultation to identify all consumers
who are to receive connections.
Once the new network is operational, NWSA should be required to remove all surface
elements of the previous networks in order to ensure that they can not simply be&re-used to
establish another new illegal connection or system that may pose a threat to the health of any
user, and which may reduce the level of service to other paying consumers.
Such action would also send a signal to potential illegal connectors of NWSA's intent to
prevent the proliferation of illegal connections, especially if there are subsequent sweeps by the
O and M division aimed at eliminating illegal connections.

7.2.1     Establishment of Environment and Safety Officer (ESO)
NWSA does not have an established schedule of responsibilities and training on matters
relating to the environment, nor does it have line responsibility to which all levels of staff owe
accountability. This has just started at the EPC with technical assistance from the Dutch
Govermnent. For this project, therefore, an Environmental Safety Officer (ESO), with a
thorough knowledge of site conditions and safety practices, should be appointed to the project
management unit (PMU). The ESO costs are shown in Table 7.4, his responsibilities are
outlined in Table 7.2 and indicative TOR for this position are included as Annex 7.1 to this
section. During this project (until the project completion report is finalised) he will be directly
responsible for ensuring the implementation of the environmental management plan. He will
also be responsible for liaising with all parties involved in the project, especially the
engineering team and environmental consultants, and advise as necessary on all environmental
matters concerning the project and in relation to the EMP.
Table 7.4    ESO Costs
Item                          Total         Sana'a 1     Long-Term
ESO                          40,000         16,000           24,000
Transportation Allowance      2,000            800            1,200
Total                        42,000         16,800           25,200
The project ESO will report directly to the PMU Director. The ESO will be responsible for
monitoring all compliance with EMP directives. Following is a breakdown of ESO responsi-
bilities.
7.2.2     Establishment of Baseline Conditions
Baseline conditions for the programme wlll be established during the pre-construction phase
with relevant measurements taken to establish, as far as is possible, the ambient conditions.
The programme of works should ensure that the baseline conditions defined represent actual
conditions for 1998/99. Each parameter will be measured at least once a month for a three-
month period prior to work starts in each area.
Y9701                                 -7.4-                            March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services      Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
W*hile this may appear to be an obvious requirement, it is often the case that projects are
identified, developed and implemented at a pace that allows only the most rudimentary
assessment of ambient conditions to be made. In these circumstances, it is not uncommon for
serious sampling / data collection, and interpretative errors to be made that provide a false
image of the ambient environment. To ensure that this does not occur, it is proposed that
preparatory works be initiated in April 1999.
All testing will be contracted out to Sana'a University, and monitored by the ESO. Costs
associated with establishing baseline conditions are found in Table 7.5. Costs will be incurred
in YR. A more detailed breakdown of these costs can be found in Annex 7.2 to this section.
The associated ongoing monitoring programme is described in Section 7.3.1.

7.2.3     Public Meetines
As outlined in Section 6.2, a public consultation programme has already been undertaken,

additional public meetings in the Akamah and Hasabah areas to inform the public of what they

treatment plant. This office will be established prior to construction activities in order that it be
fully operational when cesspit sewage starts being loaded into the treatment plant.
In addition, $55,000 will be made available for the repair/replacement of the standby diesel
generator at the existing WWTP.

7.3.1    Monitoring Programme
Physical benefits of the programme should be determined through a groundwater quality-
monitoring programme. It is claimed that project benefits may be derived from reduced
contamination of groundwater in the study area.
A monitoring programme of the ground water is being undertaken by the NWSA PMU for the
Sana'a Water Supply and Sanitation Project and the Faculty of Engineering at the Sana'a
University. This programme needs to be expanded and modified to meet the requirements of
this monitoring programme. Initially it is proposed to identify three wells for monitoring
purposes within the (Phase 1) study area. These should be the subject of a monthly monitoring
programme starting at least 3 months4 before construction activities occur in the immediate
area of influence. This will allow pre project baseline conditions to be established. Ultimately a
network of eight wells should be established covering all the project areas as follows.
-     Akamah                    2 wells (Phase 1)
-     Sawad Hanash              I well
-     Safiyah Gharbia           1 well
-     Saba'een                  I well
-.    Safiyah Sharkia           2 wells
Hasabah                   I well (Phase 1)
These wells should be monitored for microbiological and chemical quality, and depth to water
as indicated in Table 7.7. The limited number of parameters selected is a function of the cost
of laboratory analyses.
It will be noted in Table 7.7 that five wells are defined for monitoring, with the additional two
located downstreamn of the WWTP. Clearly, the vast majority of impacts associated with these
sites will be derived from the operation of the new treatment plant. However, it is argued here
4 The programme is tied to constuction works as the new system will become opertiaal; sectiot by section
on an as and when completed basis. Given the very tight project implementation Programme outlined in
Section 3, the location of the wells may be influenced by the constrction work programme.

that ultimately a comprehensive network of groundwater monitoring sites will be required, and
that such programmes should be established as soon as is possible. Therefore, if no proposals
exist for a monitoring project (associated with the WWTP Project) they should be established
within the Framework of Sana'a 1 and the wider framework of the Sector DP.

Similar arguments apply to the wastewater effluent, however the new treatment plant will have
a well-equipped and fully staffed lab and flow measurement devices. This lab will monitor the
quantity and quality of effluent from the WWTP. The ESO will work with WWTP personnel
to ensure that their analysis is available as part of the monitoring progranmme. In this context,
there is still a justifiable need within the project framework to:
- ensure that the shock loading received at the WWTP from the concentrated sludge and
slurry extracted from the cesspits5 do not have an adverse impact on the new WWTP
treatment performance (as reflected in effluent quality).
- ensure that all such wastes are processed by the WWTP and not simply discharged,
untreated.
- ensure that the wastes discharged and re-utilised meet the standards required.
a)    Site Access
For the Construction Monitoring Programme to be effective it will be necessary, during the
course of construction and operation, for authorised agents from key agencies, (NWRA,
NWSA and EPC) to occasionally conduct inspections for the purpose of determining
S This will apply mainly to project elements constrced after Akamab.
Y9701                                 -7.7-                            March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services       Sana a I Environmental Impact Assessment
compliance with the EMP and all applicable regulations and statutes. Access to all sites related
to the project must therefore be guaranteed.
Accordingly, the contract documents and operating agreements shall incorporate a phrase with
similar intent to that outlined below.
Any officer authorised in writing by EPC, NfWA or NWSA may at any time enter any
premises whether prescribed or otherwise, and may:
-  Examine and inspect equipment, control equipment, monitoring equipment or plant;
-  Take samples of any pollutants that are emitted, discharged or deposited or are likely to
be or are of a class or kind that are usually emitted, discharged or deposited from such
premises;
-  Examine any books, records or documents relating to the performance or use of such
equipment, control equipment, monitoring equipment or plant or relating to the emission,
discharge or deposit from such premises;
-  Photograph such premises as he considers necessary or make copies of any book, records
or documents seen in the course of such examination.
b)    Site Inspections
Objectives
Regular and frequent site inspections will be required to permit the ESO to monitor the
performance of the contractor, and at a later date system operators, with regard to compliance
with the stipulations contained in this EMP and the EIA.
Site inspections should be carried out on a regular basis but not necessarily to a structured
pattern. However during construction, as a minimum, the programme outlined in Table 7.8
should be observed.
To facilitate inspections, a checklist of items to be considered similar to that provided in
Appendix F shall be drawn up. The checklist should be distributed to all parties concerned
with construction, who should also receive a briefing by ESO prior to initiation of construction
works.

c)    Environmental Quality Monitoring
* Objectives
In addition to the site inspections it will also be necessary to monitor the physical environment
of the construction sites. This will require quantitative assessments derived from monitoring
sites:
-  to monitor alterations in existing physical, chemical and biological characteristics of the
environment.
-  to determine whether any detected changes in environmental components are caused by
the project or natural occurrences.
-  to determine the impacts of non compliance with ELA and EMP requirements by the
contractor, in particular to monitor emissions and discharges and ensure compliance with
local, national and international standards.
-  to deternine the effectiveness of the ameliorating measures.
-  to highlight areas of concern unforeseen in the EIA and EMP and provide a basis for
recommending further amelioration measures.
* Sampling Programme
(i)    Complaint based and Random Sampling
Random sampling, of both fixed sites and construction sites will be undertaken together with
measurements prompted by complaints by local residents. In general terms it is expected that
such works will be confined to dust and noise nuisance but capacity may be required for other
assessments. Clearly the nature and extent of this programme can not be determined but an
indicative random programme is drawn up below to which a 20% contingency is to be applied
for complaint based works. A summary of the proposed random sampling programme for the
Construction period is outlined in Table 7.9.
Table 7.9: Summarm of Proposed Sampling Programme Frequency: Construction
Parameter       Unit        Frequency        Duration      No. of Sites
. Dust       TSP        2 day / quarter   24 hour period     2
Noise    |    LeL        2 day / quarter   0600-2200          5
(ii)   Fixed Site Sampling
No fixed sampling programme is proposed. The project does not involve large-scale
earthworks or the opening up of new rights of way. Moreover, except for that originating
from off site facilities such as Pre-Fabrication yards and Borrow Pits, it is likely that the
disamenity suffered by populations in any one area will be for only a very limited period. At off
site facilities, random sampling, inspections (and complaints) will indicate whether further
actions need be taken.
All testing will be contracted out to Sana'a University, and monitored by the ESO. Costs

7.3.2     Environmental Audits
(i)    Objectives
The environmental audit process provides an assessment of the project, environmental
management activities and the effectiveness of the system in fulfilling GOY environmental
policy. In implementing the audit schemes, project areas perceived as having the highest
environmental risk are stressed. The audit programme has the responsibility to:
- examine compliance with regulatory requirements
-  examine line management systems, plant operations, monitoring practices etc.
- identify current and potential environmental problems especially during the operational
phase of the project
- check the predictions in the EIA and assure the implementations and application of
- recommended practices and procedures.
- make recommendations for the improvement of the management system of the operation.
(ii)   Frequency
At least one audit should be carried out for every major facility in operation during each
construction phase' or at a minimum of one per year, preferably during IDA supervision
missions. Qualifying facilities would include the main construction camp and any off site
processing and storage facilities.
(iii)  Implementation
All audits will be performed by the ESO, with assistance from World Bank project supervision
personnel (TOR for the environmental aspects of IDA supervision rnissions are in Appendix
G). All audits should be straightforward exercises that should take no more than one day to
6 Phase 1= Akamah and Hasabah. Phase 2 iicludes all other project components.
Y9701                                 -7.10-                           March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services       Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
complete. Accordingly, an additional $1,000 will be allocated for printing and dissemination
purposes only.
7.3.3     Public Consultation
The public consultation and awareness program comprises three components: (i) public
meetings; (ii) newspaper and television articles and programs; and (iii) household surveys.
Meetings organised by the ESO will be held semi-annually before and during the construction
period, coinciding with IDA supervision missions whenever possible. Feedback elicited during
these meetings will be used by the ESO in the semi-annual Environmental Reports. In this
way, any necessary changes to the mitigation plan will be incorporated.
7.3.4     Additional Proiect Requirements
a)    NGO Involvement
It is also important that, wherever possible, the project helps develop the capacity of NGOs.
At this stage, as with all projects, it is difficult to define NGOs that could be directly supported
in the programme. It will be the responsibility of the PMU and the ESO to identify and
encourage NGO involvement.
b)    Health Clinic
As stated in 6.3.2, as part of the project it may be necessary to establish a health clinic (within
an existing facility) dealing with populations from affected areas. Clinics should be established
in cases where project construction is taking place in areas that are not readily served by local
health facilities. Determination of this need will be the responsibility of the contractor, with
guidance from the ESO. Implementation will be done by the ESO and the PMU. Potential
costs for the clinic are US$24,800; details for which can be found in Table 7.17 of Annex 7.2.
c)    Potential Additional Costs to the Contmctor
Table 7.19 in Annex 7.2 represents costs that will be directly incurred by the contractor during
project irnplementation, in order to conform to the Environmental Management Plan. Some of
these costs (such as those for water storage) may not be necessary, but project funds will be
made available for these purposes, if and when they occur.
Until the plant is decommissioned, an office at the existing WWTP wilt be maintained, in order
to ensure that wastes from project activities are discharged directly into the inlet sump of the
treatment plant, and to make sure that fly tipping doesn't occur. All trucks entering the facility
will be recorded, and logs will be checked against those taken at the construction site. The
office will ensure that all wastes will be tansferred during off-peak lfours.

7.4.1     Monitoring
The operation of the Sana'a 1 project is entirely by gravity in pipes. There are no electro-
mechanical facilities, maintenance yards or any other site developed specifically for the project.
There will therefore be no requirement for site inspections during this phase.
Post construction monitoring for the project will be maintained with the following objectives
in mind:
(i)    to  monitor alterations in existing physical, chemical, biological and social
characteristics of the environment.
(ii)   to determine whether any detected changes in environmental components are caused
by the project or natural occurrences.
(iii)   to monitor emissions and discharges at the new wastewater treatment plant and
ensure compliance with local, national and international standards.
(iv)   to determine the effectiveness of the ameliorating measures.
(v)    to provide early warning of any potentially serious problems
(vi)   to measure long term impacts
There are no potentially significant negative residual impacts identified in the EIA that may
need to be monitored. There are however a number of potential benefits claimed for the
project that may be quantifiable. The ESO will be responsible for post-construction monitoring
until IDA's project closes in September 2002. After that, NWSA will be responsible for
carrying on the monitoring program.
7.4.2     Public Consultatioh
a)    Public Meetings
As a follow-up to civil works, and as a component of the End of Project Report, the ESO will
organise a public meeting. Emphasis will be put on lessons learnt during construction.
b)    Surveys
* Methodology
Fieldwork was conducted in Sana'a in March of 1998, principally in the neighbourhoods of
Akamah and Sawad Hanash and a total of 520 households were surveyed over a 5-day period.
Z9701                                 - 712-                           March 1999



Sana'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
Water vendors, realtors, shopowners, and local clinics were also interviewed. A total of 15
private taps were selected and sampled, and the existing treatment plant and the fields
currently irrigated with raw sewage were inspected.
* Follow-up Surveys
The Project plans follow-up surveys in both the Akamah and Hasabah areas halfway through
the Project's implementation period, which is estimated to be one year after the sewage
network has been completed. Among hypotheses to be tested, the surveys would examine the
following:
- Households take a number of defensive actions to compensate for the lack of reliability of
the water system.
- Households perceive the water coming from the water system to not be potable.
- Households take a number of preventive actions to mitigate water quality problems (e.g.
boiling water, purchasing bottled water)
- Households perceive that the major issue with water quality is contamination of faecal
nature.
-  Households would be willing to use recycled wastewater (that would meet potable
standards) for a variety of purposes, including drinking, washing, toilet flushing, and
garden irrigation.
- Significant expenditures are incurred to treat waterborne diseases.
- Households incur significant expenses to operate and maintain existing on-site sanitation
systems.
-  Households buy 50% of their water from non-NWSA sources.
- With the installation of the sewer network, property values increased by 20%.
The survey's implementation will be the responsibility of the ESO. Table 7.18, in Annex 7.2
summarises the associated costs.
Y9701                                   -7.13-                            March 1999



Annex 7.1
Terms of Reference for Environment and Safety Officer



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services      Sana a I Environmental Impact Assessment
Annex 7.1
Terms of Reference for Environment and Safety Officer
a)    Construction: Inspections and Audits
The ESO will have responsibility for environmental audits, which should be contracted out
to responsible agencies, or individuals, on a commercial basis. Independent reports of audit
findings will be prepared as part of the contract.
b)    Environmental Quality Monitoring
The ESO will contract, and directly supervise and manage contractors, such as the Faculty of
Engineering at the Sana'a University, which will be employed to carry out the required
monitoring and testing programmes, and provide the assessment reports. Each report should
contain as a minimum the following sections:
-     sampling, methodologies, equipment calibration reports, other background material
-     the empirical findings
-     statements of any extreme events or incidents reported that may abnormally influence
the empirical findings
-     analysis of the findings highlighting any changes of significance and possible, probable
causes of change
-     recommendations on actions to be taken
-     follow up on any previous recommendations
All reports prepared should be submitted to the ESO and EPC, and circulated to NWSA,
NWRA and other relevant agencies, NGOs etc.
c)    Construction Monitoring
Inspection Reporting
After each inspection a report shall be compiled that is location and activity specific, and
which identifies areas of contractor -non-compliance with the EM?, and which provides
guiding remarks on actions to be taken. The significance of the non-compliance shall also be
noted. These reports shall be prepared by the ESO. Copies of these reports shall be sent to the
EPC, the supervising engineer, and the Contractor for their action.
Every six months the inspection reports shall be compiled into a review document that shall
highlight any areas of persistent negligence by the contractor. This document shall also contain
records of any communications between the supervising engineer, ESO and the Contractor on
matters relating to the environment.
Audit Reports
Audit reports shall be prepared following each audit. These will be submitted by the sub-
contractor to the ESO. The ESO shall review the documents and circulate to the site owners
Y9701                                  714-                            March 1999



Sana'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services       Sana a I Environmental Impact Assessment
and operators with any additional recommendations from the PMU included. The reports
should comprise:
-     Background data, time, facility, operations underway, etc.
-     Statement of Findings of the Audit.
-     Statement of compliance with the recommendations of the previous audit. -
-     Recommendations for future action.
As with all reports from contractors received by the ESOJPMU, the originals will be filed and
held, available for reference by any concerned party.
Semi-Annual Environmental Reports
The ESO will be responsible for the preparation of semi-annual Environmental Reports. These
reports should be produced throughout the construction period. They will be prepared
following the World Bank semi-annual supervision missions, and based in part on the findings
of the environmental audit and consultation processes. It is envisaged that each report will
have two main sections:
I)    Review of Project Performance
This will outline the performance of all agencies involved in the Project; improvement or
deterioration, as measured against previous baselines and established targets where
appropriate; and establish new performance targets.
In the latter context, it is recommended that appropriate, specific targets be employed in the
assessment process to ensure that the findings permit direct comparison with previous
performance and in the context of defined objectives. If a specific evaluation technique or
framework is not adopted, annual targets for achievement will need to be defined for the
upcoming year in the annual report.
The assessment should draw on the findings of the inspection reports, monitoring programmes
and environmental audits.
II)   Review of Project Works
This would comprise a review of all actions undertaken within the Project in the previous
period. It would include as a minimum:
-     Progress reports on project and programme implementation
-     Comparison to Baseline Indicators
-     Summary report on training programmes I workshops held etc.
-     Activity report on the awareness campaign
-     Activity report of site inspections programme
-     Activity report from the monitoring and audit programme
In general terms each report should contain:
- background to the project /programme I works
- summary statements of methodologies adopted
Y9701                                 -7.15-                           March 1999



Sana'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services         Sana 'a I Environmental ImpactAssessment
- summaries of findings
- analysis of environmental audits
- a statement of lessons learnt
- any new mitigation measures to be implemented
- recommendations for future works
End of Project Report
On completion of project construction works, the ESO should prepare an end of project
monitoring review report. This shall contain:
- background to the project
- the empirical findings of the monitoring programme and the site inspections
- a statement on the methodologies adopted for monitoring, the suitability of equipment
utilised, its performance, the practical and technical difficulties experienced in collecting
and analysing the data. Recommendations for future works
- a statement on suitability of resources available for monitoring and inspection and
reconmmendations for future works
- a statement on the efficiency, or otherwise, of mitigation measures proposed
- a statement on the significance of any changes identified, both physical and social, as
compared to those predicted
- a statement of lessons to be learnt and recommendations on any actions to be taken to
ensure that these lessons are translated into positive actions on future projects
- a surnmary statement of the overall impact on the environment of the construction phase.

APPENDIX C
- Existing WWTP and Mitigation Plan



San'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services        Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Asesiment
APPENDIX C: EXISTING WWTP AND MITIGATION PLAN
History
The WWTP was designed to handle the future sewage flow collected by the sewerage
network and its extensions. The WWTP was located to the west of Sana'a Airport, but due to
difficulties pertaining to the site and since Stage I Sewerage Network was built, and a
temporary wastewater treatment facility (TWWTF) was constructed at Al-Rawdhah in 1988.
This TWVWTF has been referred to as the existing WWTP.
The TWWTF was designed as oxidation ponds with a design capacity of 6,000 m3/day and
sewage strength of 460 mg BOD/I. No flow measurement devices were installed and no flow
measurements were undertaken.
Since its construction, there has been no major improvement made to the TWW'T except
deepening the anaerobic bonds to accommodate more sludge.
Operational Practices
The use of such treatment facilities is restricted to small communities and/or flow and should
be located at least one Idlometre upstream of urban areas in the prevailing wind direction.
The city, since 1998, has expanded in the vicinity of the TWWTF. At the same time, more
sewage collection networks were built.
The new networks increased the flow entering the TWWTF without any modification to it.
This has reduced the retention time with an accompanying deterioration in the quality of the
effluent.
Storm water drainage facilities are limited in Sana'a, and where they exist do not often
function properly. It is common practice in Sana'a that citizens open the sewerage manhole
covers to let the storm water evacuate through the sanitary sewer system. The flow in such
cases is very high, and far beyond the capacity of the TWWTF. The operators close the inlet
to the facility and divert the whole flow to the conveyor downstreamn of the TWWTF.
The purpose of the diversion is multi-fold: to divert the large flow from the facility, to prevent
the ingress of mud, sand and debris into the facility and most importantly, not to over dilute
the liqueur in the pond and offset the microbiological balance.
Capacity
Flow
There are no flow measurements either at the inlet or outlet of the TWWTF. Therefore, there
are no records available on the flow patterm.
The flow was estimated in 1991 at 8,000 m3/day by Harvey F. Ludwig (Report on Appro-
priate Sewage Treatment for Sana'a, Seatec International Co., Ltd., October 1991).
Y9701                                 -C.-                             March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services        Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Asressment
Howard Humphreys & Partners Ltd. measured the flow continuously for 10 days, at the inlet
weir to the pumping station in March 1994, and found the average flow to be 10,197 m3/day
(Design Report and Review of Impact, January 1995).
The Consultants estimate the flow to be 15,600 m3/day, based on the current (1998)
population served and per capita water consumption.
Sewage Strength
The laboratory of NWSA Sana'a Branch has taken and analysed samples of incoming sewage
at the TWWTF at various times since its commission but at irregular times and infrequently.
The average of these samples is about 490 mg BOD/1.
Harvey F. Ludwig measured the BOD of the influent in 1991 to be 500 mg/I.
Howard Humphreys & Partners Ltd. measured the sewage strength on samples collected three
times per day on four weekly occasions in March and July 1994. The average BOD of 20
samples collected under this programme was 485.5 mg/I.
The Consultants measured the sewage strength on a composite sample taken at the TWWTF
inlet in September 1997 and September 1998 and found it to be 385 mg/l and 480 mg/I
respectively.
Effluent
The BOD of the effluent as measured by the laboratory of NWSA Sana'a Branch on samples
collected by them since the TWWTF was commissioned ranged from 48.5 to 361 mg/ with an
average of 133 mg/I.
Harvey F. Ludwig measured the BOD of the effluent in 1991 and it was about 80 mg/I.
The Consultants measured the BOD of the effluent of the TWWTF inlet in September 1998
and found it to be 150 mg/I.
It is worth noting that although there was no major change in the strength of the raw sewage,
there has been appreciable change, since 1993, in the quality of the effluent as indicated in the
following table:
Influent mg BODA               Effluent mg BODA
Period          Average   Maximum   Minimum    Average   Maximum   Minimum
1986 to 1992     536        860       340        98        135        48
1993to 1994      462        700       210        151       361        85.
1995 to 1997     480        560       205        145       202        80
1992to 1997      490        860       205        133       361        48
Y9701                                  - C2 -                             March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services     Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
Estimated Flow
The flow into the TWWTF has gradually increased since its commission, through an increase
in the number of inhabitants served and/or an increase in the per capita water consumption.
There are no records available that can assist in estimating the current flow into the TWWTF.
Flow in 1991
Harvey F. Ludwig estimated the number of equivalent connections at 13,200 with an average
of 7 persons per connection or 98,000 total served population. This gave rise to a total flow
of 8,000 m3/day or an 86 l/c/d net sewage flow.
The results of the surveys that the Consultants conducted under the Project indicate that the
average number of persons connected per one water supply house connection is 14.6. These
results also indicate that the average per capita water consumption is 65 I/d, which gives rise
to an average net sewage flow of 52 I/c/d. Based on available data, the best estimate of the
total population in 1991 is 825,000. The Consultants estimate that the population served by
the sewerage network in 1991 was 175,000, or about 12,000 connections. This population
would generate sewage flow of 9,100 m3/day.
Flow in 1994
Two basic data were measured and fixed in 1994. They were: population as per 1994 Census,
and sewage flow as per the measurement of Howard Humphreys & Partners Ltd.
Under the probable assumption that there was no increase in the population connected since
1991, a per capita net sewage flow of 58 I/d would result.
The census estinates that the portion of the population served by a sewerage system at 25
percent. The total population served in this case was estimated at about 238,600, which
results in a per capita net sewage flow of 43 Vd and 16,343 connections. The number of
connections in this case is probably exaggerated.
Flow in 1998
The Consultants estimated the population of Sana'a in 1998 at 1,196,652 people, based on the
1994 Census and population growth rate. They also estimated the population served, based
on the collected data on the expansion of the sewerage network, to be 297,600 people or
20,384 connections (14.6 persons per connection).
NWSA estimates the number of connections at 18,500, which will result in a population
served of 270,100 (14.6 persons per connection).
The generated flow is estimated to range between 11,614 m3/day (low consumption and low
population) to 15,475 m3/day (highr consumption and high population).
Y9701                                - C.3 -                          March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Serviees      Sana 'a I Enviwmmental Impact Assess. en!
Summary
The population served and the estimated flows in 1991, 1994 and 1998 are summarised in the
following table:
Year  Population  Net Sewage  Estimated Flow, Comments
Served    Flow, l/c/d    m3/day
1991    98,000       89            7,840     Low 1991
1991   175,000       52            9,100     High 1991
1994   175,000       58           10,197*    Probable 1994
1994   238,612       43           10,197*    Census based, probably exaggerated
1998   297,600       52           15,475     Study's 1998 base case
1998   297,600       43           12,797     High pop served low consumption
1998   270,100       52           14,045     Low pop served high consumption
1998   270,100       43           11,614     Low pop served low consumption
* measured flow
Summary
In summary, the actual capacity of the TWWTF, the actual flow entering the facility, and the
effectiveness of the treatment are all unknown. It is clear from the little data and information
available, that the flow entering the TWWTF far exceeds it capacity, the retention time is short
and the treatment is not very effective. BOD removal has dropped from about 82 percent in
early years of facility operation to about 68 percent in recent years.
The best estimate for the average flow is about 15,500 m3/day, and an average BOD of about
480 mg/I. The BOD of the effluent is 150 mg/l on the average.
Loading from Akamah
Pit Loading
The total volume of sewage and slurry to be removed from cesspits in the Akamah area is
30,000 m3 to be removed within 14 months of actual construction operations.  This is
equivalent to about 2,145 m3 per month or about 85 m3 per day assuming 25 working days per
month. At worst about 90 m3 would be removed daily. This constitutes about 0.6 percent of
the average daily flow into the TWWTF and 1.5 percent of the design capacity. The volume
'to be collected from the cesspits in Akamah is very small compared to the flow entering the
TWWTF and its strength does not differ from that of the flow. The effect of this added
sewage is very negligible and any shock loading is therefore not anticipated.
Temporary Collection
The total potential number of cesspits to be cleared from the Akamah area is 2,900, at an
average rate of 8.28 pits per worldng day. The population of Akamah is 41,000 persons
connected to about 3,500 pits, or 11.7 persons per pit on average. The 'sewage should be
collected in temporary facilities from houses that are to be disconnected from their cesspits
and are awaiting connection to the sewage collection network. The. waste of about 50 persons
should be collected in such cases. At 52 1/c/d net wastewater return, the volume to be
Y9701                                -C.4 -                            March 1999



Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Services      Sana a I Environmental Imoact As. e.tunent
collected and removed is 2.6 m3/day. This constitutes about 0.017 percent of the average
daily flow into the TWWT.
Permanent Connection
The total population of Akamah will be connected within 14 months construction period. This
means that wastewater from about 2,930 persons per month will be collected and disposed of
through the permanent sewage collection network. The average daily flow added to the
systems would be about 150 m3/day every month. The actual construction operations are
expected to commence in August 1999. Therefore the average daily additional sewage flow at
the end of each month is expected as follows:
Month           Flow, m3/day      Month           Flow, m3/day
August 1999         150           March 2000         1,200
September 1999      300           April 2000         1,350
October 1999        450           May 2000           1,500
November 1999       600           June 2000          1,650
December 1999       750           July 2000          1,800
January 2000        900           August 2000        1,950
February 2000      1,050          September 2000     2,100
Under this condition, about 1 percent of the average daily flow into the TWWTF is perma-
nently added to the sewage collection system.
The new WWIP is scheduled to be commissioned in December 1999. By that time, about five
percent of the average daily flow into the TWWTF should have been permanently comected.
Options for Mitigation
Based on the above presentation, the options for mitigation are the following:
* collect the flow generated from the project area and from temporary collection, and the
cesspit contents, and discard to a tenporary site.
* overload the TWWTF further by the sewage collected from the project area and through
temporary collection.
* discharge the cesspit contents and from the temporary collection into the existing sewer
system.
* discharge the cesspit contents and from the temporary collection at the TWWTF during
off-peak hours.
* upgrade the TWWTF to cater for the additional loading.
* defer the project implementation.
The BOD level from samples collected from one cesspit in Akamah area is about 490 mg/I,
which is about the average BOD level of Sana'a raw sewage. The volume removed per
cesspit and dumped into the TWWTF is about 0.8 percent of the average daily flow entering
it. This is equivalent to a dilution rate of 125 times.

APPENDIX D
Review Of Impacts



APPENDIX D:        REVIEW OF IMPACTS
This appendix is reproduced entirely as is from the report:
Sana'a Sewage Treatment Plant
Final Design
Design Report and Review of Impact
Howard Humphreys & Partners
1995



9.   REVIEW OF IMPACTS
This section reviews the impacts of the proposed treatment plant
relating to:
* Odour
4  Effluent re-use and disposal
* Sludge disposal
* Occupational hazards
* Power failure
* The airport
9.1   Odour
The treatment process selected, the crude sewage extended
aeration process, is inherently odour free, since incoming sewage,
which is likely to be septic, is stabilised by contact with the large
volume of aerated activated sludge present in the aeration tanks.
The proposed design additionally features returning the Retum
Activated Sludge to the inlet pumping station where it will mix
with the incoming sewage and significantly control odours at that
point.
The inlet pumping station screws will be covered to prevent the
release of any remaining odours.
The extended aeration process stabilises the surplus sludge by
aerobic means within the system and the resultant sludge is
basically odour free, although it does have a faint earthy smell
which is not unpleasant
The sludge drying beds will emit this "earthy" smell but it will not
cause objection or complaint as the nearest neighbouring
buildings are more than 500 metres from the location of the beds.
The Recca Sewage Treatment Works, Kuwait, is generally similar
to the proposed Sana'a Treatment Works, but treats highly septic
sewage without odour or complaint
There is no reason why Sana'a Sewage Treatment Plant should be
any less successful and free of odour.
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREP.A01                                   Page 52.



9.2  Effluent Re-Use and Disposal
9.2.] Previous Studies
The subject of effluent reuse has focused much attention in the
Republic of Yemen and there are several useful previous reports
to which reference has been made namely:
- Water Supply Nvastewater and Sanitation, Final Report in 6
volumes, June 1992 by UNDP/DESD Project YEM/88/001.
- Report on Appropriate Sewage Treatnent for Sana'a, October
1991 by Harvey F Ludwig, SEATEC International Co. Ltd,
Bangkok, Thailand.
- Sana'a Sewerage and Sewage Treatment, 1976, Howard
Humphreys and Sons.
- Sana'a Sewerage System Master Plan, December 1975,
Italconsult, Rome.
9.2.2 Physical Setting
9.2.2.1 Location of Irrigation Areas
The proposed irrigation scheme area utilising treated effluent
would be most suitably located downstream of the treatment plant
site spanning either side of Waadi Saila.
9.2.2.2 Topography
The elevation of the treatment plant site is about 2181 m above
sea level. There is a smooth fall to the waadi on both east and
west sides and the waadi bed gradient is approximately 1 in 400.
The topography is generally even to slightly undulating and is
suitable for a gravity irrigation scheme from the treatment plant
outlet. There is no detailed topographical survey of the area.
9.2.2.3 Rainfall
Annual rainfall data has been obtained for Sana'a from the Civil'
Aviation and Meteorology Authority, over years 1983 to 1989.
The annual average calculates to 180 mm, the wet season being
generally between tertiary and August peaking in April. The
remaining months are dry except for light showers. The average
monthly rainfall is given in Table 9.2.1 below.

In their 1975 Master Plan Italconsult estimated the mean annual
rainfall to be 300 mm between 1978 and 1974. They also
estimated the 1 in 100 year 24 hour rainfall intensity to be 97
mm.
Invariably a months rainfall can occur in one or two heavy falls
giving rise to waadi spate flow which is typical of the region. This
rainfall therefore cannot be considered as effective in the
determination of crop water requirements.
9.2.2.4 Flooding
In 1975 Italconsult undertook a study of the surface water
resources of the catchments surrounding Sana'a. They estimated
the 1 in 100 year flows entering the city from the south to be 52
m3/s.
Historic floods have been recorded in 1964, 1967, 1970, 1975 and
as recently as March 1994. Although the WVaadi Saila watercourse
through Sana'a has sufficient capacity to carry most floods,
flooding in the city was recorded in 1975 as well as 1994.
To alleviate flooding in the city Italconsult recommended that
Waadi Saila be canalised over a total distance of 8.6 km from
south of the city north to the existing treatment plant. They
estimated the cumulative total 1 in 100 year flood to be 208 m3/s
in the waadi north of the city and then recommended further
study of flood flows up to the airport and beyond.
The existing waadi capacity north of the proposed new treatnent
plant has been estimated to be 250-300 m3/s assuming an average
width of 80 metres and depth of 1.5 metre, a slope of 1 in 400
and Mannings roughness of 0.03. Farmers have already built
some bunding in the area to protect their crops against flooding.
9.2.2.5 Geology and Soils
The geology of the area comprises Quaternary Volcanics and
fluviatile deposits which overlie tertiarytrap volcanics to the south
and east and Mesozoic and Palaeocene sedimentary formations to
the north and west. The thickness of the Quaternary sediments
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREP.AO1                                   Page 54.



may be considerable.
The soils have been examined in outcrop and in trial pits. In
general the top layer ranges from 0.7 m to 3.7 m comprising a
dark brown firm to stiff clayey silt with medium sized gravel.
These soils have good infiltration rates but have sufficient clay
content to cause ponding when saturated.
Photographic evidence of existing cropping in the waadi shows
the soils to have reasonable nutrients although short of nitrogen.
It is reported that farmers use urea but there is no data on
quantities of use.
9.2.3 Effluent Re-Use Standards
9.2.3.1 General
Re-using wastewater usually incurs some risk to human health
and the environment. These risks must be reduced to a level
acceptable to the community and weighed against the benefits of
alleviating water shortages.
The re-use of treated wastewater can only be considered where
the allocation of adequate economic and manpower resources
ensure that:
- treatment levels can be maintained;
- monitoring of water quality and health impacts continue;
- the importance of adhering to the necessary restrictions on
usage.
These requirements are essential. There is no sense in re-
exposing the population to the diseases which the very collection
and treatment of waste water is intended to prevent.
The primary consideration in the selection of treatment process
must be the efficacy of pathogen removal. Those pathogens likely
to be present in waste water must be assessed for each particular
area. It must be realised that much uncertainty still exists
regarding precise removals of various organisms and indeed
whether such organisms are good indicators of actual patiogen
content.
Reliability in performance is probably the most important factor
in any treatment process. Second to this must be operability, i.e.
the local skills, maintenance facilities and adequate funds for
running costs must be adequate to ensure efficient operation of
the wastewater supply.
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREP.AO1                                     Page 55.



The reuse of treated sewage effluent for agriculture is not a new
concept. Effluent reuse for landscape greening is common place
in many countries notably the Middle East. Several countries have
developed their own effluent treatment standards and
bacteriological limits for reuse.
9.2.3.2 Health Aspects
A WHO Scientific Group on Health Aspects of the Use of Treated
Wastewater for Agriculture and Aquaculture met in Geneva in
November 1987 and the report was published two years later
(WHO, 1989). The approach to the health effects of effluent re-
use taken by this group has been adopted as a basis for
considering the health aspects of effluent re-use in many
countries. WHO's recommended guidelines given in the report
are shown in Table 9.2.2.

9.2.3.3 Guidelines for Interpretation of Water Quality for Irrigation
It is important to understand the chemical reaction between the treated effluent
supply and the soils which are being irrigated. Wastewater contains impurities,
and therefore careful consideration must be given to the possible long term
effects on soils and plants from salinity, sodicity, nutrients and trace elements
that occur naturally or are added during domestic or industrial use. Such
effects are normally manageable if associated problems with these impurities
are understood and allowances made for them.
The guidelines presented in Table 9.2.3 have been reproduced from FAO Paper
No. 29. They are sufficient to make reliable estimates of soil and crop
responses to the use of treated effluent where the primary limitation is the
chemical constituent, such as the total dissolved salts, relative sodium content
and toxic ions.

The above table shows the constituent limits using domestic

chemicals and the degree of dilution of industrial effluent before
making a decision for reuse of such waters. At present industrial
effluent from Sana'a is believed to be insignificant.
9.2.4 Treated Effluent Supplies
9.2.4.1 Treatment Process Design
The design of the treatment plant has been described in detail in
Section 4 of this design report. The important parameters at the
plant outlet will be BOD better than 30 mgl and SS better than 30
mgl with disinfection to reduce faecal coliforms below 200 per
100 ml, which is within WHO guidelines for the irrigation of all
categories of crops given on Table 9.2.2, except in respect of
removal of Intestinal Nematodes which may not always be
achieved.
9.2.4.2 Quantities of Treated Effluent for Re-use
The new plant will be designed to an ultimate capacity of 50,000
m3/day, i.e. 580 I/s average with flows varying between a normal
daily peak of 1100 I/s down to 180 I/s using the same diurnal
flow profile as the existing plant The diurnal variation in flow
would be significant and. consideration of storage would--be
required to suit irrigation practice.
It is possible that the new plant will be built to provide a capacity
of 25,000 m3/day (290 I/s) as a first stage.
Irrigation development should accordingly be staged to suit the
phasing of the plant.
9.2.5 Agiculture
9.2.5.1 .Cropping
There are four key issues to be addressed when using treated
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREP.AO1                                   Page 61.



effluent for irrigated agriculture.
- The supply is generally constant throughout the year and
without costly storage being provided perennial crops need to
be considered in order to match consumption with supply.
- Crop selection should take into account the chemical quality of
the effluent and the potential health risks as set out in the FAO
and WHO guidelines.
- Operation and maintenance of the scheme should be 'kept
simple to ensure human contract vith the effluent is kept to a
minimum.
- Gravity methods of furrow and basin are preferable to
pressurised systems.
In addressing the above issues consideration is given to the
following perennial crops as most suitable to be irrigated.
- Trees - orchards fruit trees, citrus, nuts, eucalyptus and conifer
for woodlots (there is a shortage of firewood in Sana'a).
- Cereals - wheat barley sorghum
- Fodder - alfalfa, grasses for hay, etc.
Depending on market demands some annual cropping of
vegetables and legume crops could also be considered
intercropped between perennial crops.
9.2.5.2 Crop Water Requirements
a) Potential Evapotranspiration (Eto)
The Surface Vater Resources Final Report 1992 by UNDP gives
potential Evapotranspiration (Eto) -calculated using the Penman
method for several centres throughout the Republic of Yemen.
The annual total for Sana'a was calculated to he .2-050 mm but
no figures were available on a monthly basis.
By using data from the Civil Aviation and Meteorology Authority
averaged over the period of record 1983 to 1989, we have
undertaken an estimation of potential evapotranspiration on a
monthly basis using the Blaney Griddle method. The annual
total calculated to be 1791 mm. Penman gives a more accurate
estimate than Blaney-Griddle hence the monthly Blaney Griddle
values have been increased in the ratio of 2050/1791 as shown
in Table 9.2.5 below.

9.2.5.3  Scheme Areas
Based on the peak requirement of 1.62 I/s/ha in June the plant
supply of 290 I/s in Stage I would irrigate 179 ha. But this only
occurs for one month. It is more realistic to adopt a design
module of 1.26 l/s/ha being an approximate annual average,
which would give a net irrigated area of 230 ha. The reason for
this is that there would be sufficient area to accommodate
84.333.0/Aw/3113/DESREP.Aol                                              Page 63.



fluctuations and any oversupply from the plant. Also any under
supply of 20% to perennial crops in the months of May, June and
July would be unlikely to create stress problems. Hence the Stage
I irrigation area should be designed for 230 ha (250 ha gross)
extending to an ultimate size of 460 ha (500 ha gross) when the
treatment plant reaches full capacity of 580 1/s.
9.2.5.4 Existing Farming
Effluent from the existing temporary treatnent plant has been
used by local farmers to grow a corn crop in the waadi. Local
farmers adjacent to the airport are also using wells to grow
fodder crops such as alfalfa and vegetable crops. Hence there is
already experience of irrigated agriculture in the area and
particularly effluent reuse.
It is vital that any proposed formal irrigation system using effluent
from the plant should take full account of existing farming
systems, plot sizes, crop types, irrigation methods, social attitudes
and marketing aspects.
The detailed designs of a scheme would need to take full
consideration of these social issues. It is the farmers who will be
using the effluent and for maximum sustainable benefits to be
achieved full account must be taken of farmer aspirations and
needs.
9.2.5.5 Irrigation Methods
From our reconnaissance surveys, enxsting irrigation practised in
the waadi is based on channels serving 1 to 2 ha units utilising
furrow or basin methods. The topography is most suitable for
- -     surface irrigation. It is therefore proposed that similar methods
to those being used be adopted for any new scheme. The
methods are cheap, conform to existing traditions and there is no
justification to use more costy pressurised sprinkler or drip
systems.

9.2.6.1 Conveyance System
A schematic layout of a possible Stage I scheme of 250 ha (gross)
is shown in Figure 7. Design concepts are:
- The central waadi channel should remain :untouched as the
natural drainage channel.
- Bunding should be provided either side of the waadi channel to

canals to serve up to 2 ha units.

- Structures would be either reinforced concrete or block work.
- Farm access roads would be provided along all primary and
secondary canals.
The scheme has been designed on a modular basis so that
secondary blocks can be built as and when necessary in a
northerly direction, either side of the wvaadi.
9.2.6.2 Scheme Operation
The scheme is designed to operate on a continuous or rotational
flow basis. Primary and secondary-canals would maintain their
design capacity cross sections throughout to permit rotational
operation at tertiary level.
Moreover the primary canals should be designed to full capacity
to permit extension of the scheme to its ultimate size of 500 ha
(gross).
Diurnal variation of flows through the new plant must be
accommodated in storage ponds to give a controlled discharge to
the irrigation system to suite irrigation prac&iee.
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREP.A01                                   Page 65.



To cater for variation in crop water demands over the year by
storage of effluent in low demand seasons for use in high demand
seasons would require storages of approximately 1.0 million m3
for 250 ha Stage I and double this amount when the scheme
reaches full size. To provide such storage in the waadi would
require a large area, require costly spillway works and put
agricultural land out of commission.
It is therefore considered prudent to over irrigate when supplies
exceed demand. This would be unlikely harm crops and any
surplus would recharge groundwater. The porosity of the soils
would not impede drainage but the water table would need to be
carefully monitored during the life of the scheme. High water
tables could create salinity conditions with consequent crop
failures.
