9.2.7 Implications of Using Treated Effluent
9.2.7.1 Nutrients and Chemical Constituents
The nutrients in reclaimed municipal wastewaters provide
fertiliser benefits to crop development but in certain instances are
in excess of plant needs and may cause problems related to
excessive vegetative growth, delayed or uneven maturity, or
reduced quality. Periodic checking is needed to estimate the
amount of nutrients being applied. These amounts should then
be included as part of the fertiliser application.  Nutrients
occurring in quantities important to agricultural management
include nitrogen and phosphorous and occasionally potassium,
zinc, boron and sulphur. The most beneficial and the most
frequendy excessive nutrient is nitrogen.
Total nitrogen includes NH4-N, NO3-N, No2N and organic N. The
nitrogen is reclaimed wastewater that reaches the field in
irrigation water is essentially equal to fertiliser nitrogen but is not
easily regulated. At each irrigation, nitrogen is applied along with
the water and fertilises the crop. This is beneficial in the early
stages of growth but much less beneficial towards maturity. In
some cases, nitrogen can be excessive, stimulates excessive
vegetative growth, and may delay maturity or reduce crop quality.
In other cases, too litde nitrogen may be present and
supplemental fertiliser nitrogen would be needed for satisfactory
crop yields. Hence it will be important to monitor nitrogen levels
at field level in order to estimate fertiliser requirements during
operation of the Scheme.
9.2.7.2 Handling of Treated Effluent for Irrigation
In general high soil moisture contents, low temperature, high soil
Ph and the presence of organic matter will tend to increase
survival time of any pathogenic organisms contained in sewage
effluent. In spite of provision of disinfection these factors make
it all the more essential that adequate safety standards are adopted
and moreover adhered to.
Crops to be irrigated can be divided into two categories: those
84.333.0/Aw/3113/DESREP.AO1                                      Page 67.



grown for forage and fodder for animal consumption and those
for human consumption. The latter can be subdivided into crops
eaten raw and those normally cooked before being eaten.
In all cases irrigation should not be permitted for at least 7 days
prior to harvesting. In the case of forage and fodder harvesting
handling is likely to be largely mechanical, thus reducing the
hazards to farm workers. Moreover, any residual bacteria in the
effluent transferred to crops is destroyed within the animals
digestive system forming a second barrier to disease transmission
to humans.
Should vegetables be considered in addition to perennial
orchards, care should be taken to ensure they are well cooked
before eaten. In addition, drying and storing many such crops
would normally remove most pathogenic risks. Vegetables eaten
raw provide the greatest danger to human health and irrigation
with sewage effluent should be avoided. If treated effluent is used
on such crops then they should preferably be irrigated only by a
non contact system such as ridged furrows but great handling
care is needed. When the scheme is established and there is a
market demand, there are good arguments for placing greater
emphasis on the cultivation of crops requiring some form of
manufacturing process before use (i.e. tomato paste/juice,
sunflower and castor). This would reduce the need for rigorous
high standards in treated effluent quality.
9.2.7.3 Effluent Quality Control
Although the design of the treated effluent is to be governed by
prescribed WHO and FAO standards the success of any scheme
will be determined by the control of the quality and quantity of
the effluent outflow within these limits. Adequate facilities and
manpower must therefore be made available to ensure quality
control is effective continuously.
9.2.7.4 Groundwater Pollution
There are a number of wells used for irrigation and potable water
purposes located adjacent to the waadi downstream of the
treatment plant site.
Up until late 1993 the effluent from the temporary treatment
plant, which is of very poor quality, flowed in the waadi past the
new treatment plant site and downstream of the site where it was
abstracted and used for irrigation. Flows in the waadi caused
pollution of the adjacent wells, mainly, it is believed, due to
inadequate lining and sealing of the upper parts of the wells
allowing polluted groundwater to seep into the wells.
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREPA01                                     Page 68.



The wells within the zone of influence of any irrigation or effluent
disposal area should be properly lined and sealed to a depth of
around 50 metres to prevent pollution, which should be carefully
monitored.
9.3   Sludge Disposal
9.3.1 Sludge Characteristics
The proposed treatment plant will produce waste solids in the
form of sludge. At the design flows of 50,000 m3/d about 80
tonnes/day of dried sludge at a moisture content of 30% will be
produced.
The sludge will be largely stabilised by aerobic digestion within
the activated sludge plant and will be given further treatment by
thickening and drying on beds before storage/composting for 6
months in a sludge storage area.
This process of treatment will render the sludge dry, inoffensive
and odour free with the appearance of loamy soil. The treatment
and storage processes will render the sludge essentially free of
pathogens.
During storage, the sludge should be placed in windro'ws
approximately 2 metres high and turned over with a tractor
shovel from time to time to encourage the composting process.
9.3.2 Land Application
The composted sludge will be suitable for land application by
manure spreader when it will h-ave the following advantages:
* Low cost fertiliser
* Soil improvement by reducing the density of the soil
* Improved moisture retention.
The sludge will typically be 30% solids having a content of 2%
nitrogen, 1% phosphorous and 0.15% potassium.
Land application of dried sludge is a well established and
successful process used worldwide. The fertiliser is of low
strength, easily applied and has reduced nitrate leaching
characteristics.
Typical application rates used in central United States of America
are around 50-70 tonnes of composted sludge per hectare.
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREP.AO1                                   Page 69.



9.3.3 Regulating Considerations
Whenever sludge is re-used it is necessary to consider regulation
and safeguards. Sludge management is currently a matter of
considerable debate throughout the world. For example, in the
United States long-standing sludge management regulations are
currently in the process of being revised. The revisions have
been under preparation, review and debate for several years.
Consensus between the regulators and those regulated has not
been easy to achieve. In Europe, European Community directives
regarding sludge management are under development.
Despite the continuing debate regarding regulation of sludge
management throughout the world, a number of general duties
are available from this experience. Regulations will generally
address the following:
*  Pathogen destruction
'. Heavy metal content
* Toxicity of extractable material
A  Vector attraction
*  General nuisances associated with management practices.
Pathogen destruction and vector attraction are generally effectively
controlled by adequate sludge stabilisation as is proposed at the
treatment plant.
The current USA regulations provide useful guidelines for initial
planning. In those regulations, a number of processes including
composting processes have been defined in national regulations
as a "Process to Significantly Reduce Pathogens (PSRP)". Sludge
that is to be applied to land must be treated with a PSRP, human
access must be controlled for 12 months, and grazing animal
access must be prevented for at least 1 month. Sludge that is to
be applied to land that is used for producing crops for human
consumption within 18 months after application must be treated
with a 'Process to Further Reduce Pathogens (PFRP)". These
processes involve elevated temperatures that are achieved either
naturally (such as composting or thermophilic aerobic digestion)
or using external heat sources (such as heat drying or heat
treatment). Other processes can be used as PFRPs if they can be
demonstrated to provide acceptable reduction of pathogens and
vector attraction.
Heavy metals content and toxicity are primarily determined by the
portion of wastewater that comes from industries and the types of
industrial dischargers. It is currently expected that few industries
will discharge to the WWTP, so the content of heavy metals and
the toxicity potential of the sludge should be minimal.
84.333.O/AW/3113/DBSREPAO1                                    Page 70.



Appropriate regulations are needed to control the introduction of
these materials into the sewerage system if beneficial use of the
sludge is to be practised. Current USA regulations and guidelines
specifically control annual application of cadmium and cumulative
application of cadmium, copper, lead, nickel and zinc.
Control of nuisance conditions requires proper selection, design
and operation of facilities used for the various steps in sludge
management   Nuisance  conditions that are  sometimes
encountered during sludge management operations include
odour, unsighdiness, e.g., due to leaking transport vehicles, iloise
and dust.  Regulations typically require that such nuisance
conditions not occur beyond the boundary of the facility used for
sludge management operations (examples of which might include
the  VWWTP boundary, the edge of a field used for agricultural
application of sludge, or the perimeter of a sludge composting
facility).
Given certain safeguards as outlined above, agricultural utilisation
of the sludge should be of mutual benefit to agriculture and the
operation of the wastewater treatment plant
9.4   Occupational Hazards
Sewage Treatment Plants present a number of occupational
hazards which can be safeguarded against by design of the
facilities and by good working practices.
Particular hazards which may be encountered include:
*  Infection
+  Asphyxiation
*  Drowning
+  Electrocution
* Chemical hazards
9.4.1 Infection
Sewage is polluted with infectious bacteria, viruses, cysts, ova and
parasites which could be harmful to the operators if basic care is
not taken.
Operators should avoid contact with the liquid by wearing gloves
and protective footwear and by rigorous attention to personal
hygiene.
A supply of protective clothing, including laundered overalls,
should be issued to all operators who should be required to wear
them whilst employed on the site.
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREP.A01                                  Page 71.



All staff should be given instruction, both verbal and wTitten, on
health and hygiene requirements on the site.
A supply of operational and maintenance protective clothing will
be included in the contract for the construction of the treatment
plant.
In general, given reasonable precautions, sewage treatment works
operators have a low incidence of infectious diseases and this is
not a high risk.
9.4.2 Asphyxiation
Asphyxiation is a high risk in sewage treatment works due to the
presence of confined spaces in which noxious gases can build up
and operators can be overcome by fumes.
Typical risk areas include manholes, chambers, pump sumps, etc.
Rigorous procedures must be established for defining confined
spaces and for entering confined spaces. These will require that
all confined spaces be identified with notices and that entry into
these can only take place after testing for injurious gases and lack
of oxygen has been carried out All entries must be made by
teams comprising at least 2 operators, one of whom remains
outside or at the surface, equipped with adequate safety
equipment.
The supply of suitable safety equipment will be included in the
construction contract.
9.4.3 Drowning
Drowning is an occupation hazard due to the presence of tanks
and channels containing deep water.  Aeration tanks are
particularly dangerous due to the reduced density of the aerated
water which causes persons to sink.
Careful design of access and guardrailing together with awareness
by the operators are the key factors in preventing drowning.
9.4.4 Electrocution
All of the treatment units are powered by electric motors and
operated using electrical control equipment. Electricity in a wet
environment is a well known hazard which could lead to
electrocution without proper safeguards.
Safeguards include the design and specification of the electrical
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREPAol                                     Page 72.



equipment to suit the environment and with specific safety
provisions together with the training of the operators in the use
of electrical equipment.
Installation and maintenance of all electrical equipmnent should be
carried out by trained electrical specialists. Unauthorised entry
to electrical equipment should be prevented by locking devices
and strict procedures.
9.4.5 Chemicals
A number of chemicals which could be used on sewage treatment
plants are hazardous and care must be taken to ensure that
operators are made aware of these and of the precautions to be
adopted.
Chlorine is likely to be the most hazardous chemical with the
biggest risk at Sana'a SIW. The chlorine store will be provided
with gas monitoring equipment, automatic alarms and a drench
shower as safety provisions, but it is essential that the operators
of the chlorination plant are given proper training in the
procedures and safety measures to be adopted with chlorine.
This especially applies to the handling of chlorine drums, both
full and empty.
Given reasonable precautions and proper procedures chlorine is
safe and efficient as has been proved in countless thousands of
installations throughout the world.
9.5   Power Failure
The consequences of prolonged power failure include:
* Overflowing of untreated sewage into the waadi
* Breakdown of the aerobic treatment process
*  Die-off of aerobic bacteria in the activated sludge leading to
poor performance on re-starting of the electrical supply.
*  Gross pollution and odour.
The electrical load at the treatment plant is significant and for this
reason full standby generation is proposed which will
automatically start up and restore supply in case of mains failure.
9.6   Airport'
The main treatment units will be some 500 m away from the line
84.333.0/AW/3113/DESREP.A01                                   P2ge 73.



of the main runway and will not have any adverse physical effect
upon operation or safety.
Odour will be controlled and the process used will not have an
impact on the airport
Small birds are likely to be attracted by the sludge drying beds
but, with the absence of polishing ponds, large birds are unlikely
to be attracted.
Street lighting and flood lighting will be designed to avoid aerial
glare.

The following guidelines are prepared for Pre construction and Construction operations.

No building demolition works are proposed at this stage. If during the course of his works the
contractor requires to demolish a structure he must notify NWSA in good time to allow for
the necessary procedures and authorisations to be obtained.
No structure shall be demolished without approval from NWSA.
E.2       DEFINITION OF SITE ACCESS
All access routes to and within the site will be clearly marked and where they are not existing
public rights of way, clearly demarcated within as narrow a right of way as is practical given
the anticipated vehicle use characteristics.
The aligmnent and profile of construction access roads shall be subject to the approval of
NWSA. In approving the alignment, profile and ultimately the construction of such roads
during the detailed design stage, NWSA shall consider the long term potential use of the road.
In cases where no long term life is anticipated the aligmnent and profile shall be considered
bearing in tnind the ease with which the road can be retumed to its previous use or new use.
E.3       CONSTRUCTION ACIVITIES
E.3.1     General
Work shall be carried out in such a manner that there is no damage to or interference with:
* existing watercourses or drainage systems;
X utilities;
* structures, roads, or other property,
*  public or private vehicular or pedestrian access;
other than to the extent that is necessary for them to be removed or diverted to permit the
execution of the works within the defined operational perimeter.
Any facilities disturbed or damaged directly or indirectly due to the Contractor's operations
shall be restored to the original condition. The Contractor shall discuss his restoration plans
with the owners concerned prior to the start of restoration work to obtain the agreement of
the owners concerned.
The Contractor shall at all times keep the construction areas, repair shops, storage sites and
other areas under his control free from accumulation of waste material, construction debris
and rubbish. The Contractor's safety superintendent shall perform regular inspections of all
areas.

immediate future.
-    all existing facilities are, at present, carrying out the processes required to provide the

carry significant proportions of heavy goods traffic.
Accordingly, it is considered that the only significant impact will be the risk of an increase in
road accidents.
The contractor shall therefore be required to pay particular attention to ensuring that all
vehicles utilised in the transport of raw materials are well maintained, loaded appropriately and
that drivers are properly trained.

4.    Spoil Heaps, Aggregates etc.
Are spoil heaps of an appropriate size?
Are materials separated correctly?
Is there evidence of excessive wind blowing off material?
Is there evidence of turbid waters running off heaps?
5.    Trench Works
Are trench sides properly buttressed?
Are access points to trenches appropriate?
Is cut material placed away from sides of trench?
Is topsoil being salvaged and placed as specified in contract specifications?
Are men in trenches properly equipped and protected?
Is excavated material placed in discrete piles?
6.    Backfdll
Are soil and topsoil properly replaced without mixing?
Is backflll appropriately compressed?
7.    Processing Sites
Are sites correctly located to mnmse adverse atmospheric and noise pollution effects?
Are sites secure?
Are all fuel stores etc. placed on appropriately sized hard stands?
Are fuelling and maintenance of equipment conducted at defined sites?
Are HAZOPS procedures in place and are staff awar of procedures?

Are equipment washing procedures being observed?
Is there evidence of excessive wind blowing off material?
Is there evidence of turbid waters running off heaps?
Are materials stored appropriately, (e.g.Bitumen)?
8.     Clean-up
Is the final clean-up appropriately timed?
Has all man-made debris been removed?
Have all trenches been restored to as close as practicable to original configurations?
Has access to all areas been restored?

Sana 'a Water Supply and Sanitation Se,vices       Sana 'a I Environmental Impact Assessment
APPENDIX G:        SANA'A WATER SUPPLY & SANITATION PROJECT
TOR: IDA SUPERVISION MISSIONS: ENVIRONMENTAL
ASPECTS
Background. One per year, IDA supervision missions for the Sana'a Water Supply &
Sanitation Project will include a qualified Environmental Specialist (ES) as part of the
supervision team. The ES will be responsible for ensuring compliance with the Environmental
Impact Assessment (EIA) and Management Plan. While in Sana'a, he will work closely with
the Environment and Safety Officer (ESO) in the Project Management Unit (PMU) and
relevant staff of the Sana'a Resident Mission.
While on supervision mission, the ES will have the following responsibilities:
1. Environmental Audit
The first task of the ES while on mission in Sana'a will be to assist the ESO in performing the
current semi-annual Environmnental Audit. He will review the other semi-annual audit from
outside Yemen, in coordination with the IDA supervision team and the Sana'a Resident
Mission. The Environmental Audit will: (i) assess the project's environmental management
activities; and (ii) the effectiveness of the management plan in fulfiuling GOY environmental
policy. In preparing the audit, project areas perceived as having the highest environmental risk
are stressed. Specifically, the environmental audit will:
* examine compliance with regulatory requirements
* examine line management systems, plant operations, monitoring practices etc.
*  identify current and potential environmental problems especially during the operational
phase of the project
* check the predictions in the ELA and assure the implementations and application of
recommended practices and procedures
* make recommendations for improving implementation of the Management Plan
*  assess the effectiveness of implementing mitigating measures which were identified in the
Environmental Management Plan
*  assess progress on the training program at the WWTP
The ES will also review with the ESO the latest Environmental Audit completed during the
previous six months and make comments as required.
2. Public Consultation
The ES will also attend at least one public meeting about the Project while in Sana'a. These
public meetings are part on the mitigation plan's public consultation process, and are
Drganized by the ESO. They are designed to both inform the public as to the status of the
project, and to elicit feedback from members of the community who are, or may be, effected
by the project's works. It is the responsibility of the ES to ensure that the ESO has scheduled
a meeting during the time that the ES will be in Sana'a.
Y9701                                 -G.l -                            March 1999



Sana a Water Supply and Sanitation Services        Sana wa I Environmental Impact Assessment
3. Environmental Report.
Following review of the environmental audits and public meetings, the ESO and ES will work
together to produce the current semi-annual Environment Report. It is envisaged that each
report will have two main sections:
A. Review of Project Performance
This will outline the environmental performance of all agencies involved in the Project;
improvement or deterioration, as measured against baseline indicators in the EIA and
Management Plan. In addition, and if so indicated, new targets could be established.
In the case of new targets, monitoring should permit direct comparison with previous
performance and in the context of defined objectives. If a specific evaluation technique or
framework is not adopted, annual targets for achievement will need to be defined for
subsequent semi-annual environmental reports.
The assessment should draw on the findings of the inspection reports, the enviromnental
audits, public consultation, monitoring programs and environmental audits.
B. Review of Environmental Management Plan
This would comprise a review of all actions undertaken to date within the Project having to do
with the EIA and the Environmental Management Plan. It would include as a minirmum:
-     Progress reports on project and program implementation
-     Comparison to Baseline Indicators
-     Summary report on training programs / workshops held etc.
-     Activity report on the awareness campaign
-     Activity report of site inspections program
-     Activity report from the monitoring and aucht program
As a minimum, the report should also include the following information, which will be
collected during the mitigation plan's monitoring process:

4. Coordination with the Resident Mission
During the supervision mission, the ES will closely coordinate with the Sana'a Resident
Mission and keep the Resident Mission informed of all scheduled meetings. The ES will also
prepare an "Environmental Management Plan Implementation Summary" annex for the
mission's Memorandum of Understanding.
Before leaving Sana'a, the ES will brief the relevant person in the RM on results of the
mission and make suggestions on items to be monitored by the Resident Mission before the
ES's next visit. These items to be monitored will be contained in a schedule of tasks to be
performed and compared against milestones to monitor progress on project performance.
